Then place small olive cauters and more numerous large ones on the fire, and blow on them until they become thoroughly ignited.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery is broken, and place the cautery on the vein after you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
If the blood flows out when you raise your finger from the opening of the artery and extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those that are prepared in the fire.
Therefore, do not cease to do this with one after another until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and do not cause further harm to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it has not been extracted. For when it is drawn out, its ends are contracted, and the blood is severed. Or it should be tied with a strong thread. Or let medicines with the property of severing the blood be applied to it, and constrict it with a properly tight poultice.
Whoever administers its severance with a ligature and constriction with cloth, or by application of burning substances and similar methods, does not completely heal it, except rarely.
If it happens to someone and a physician or medicine is not present: then begin by placing the index finger over the opening of the vein itself, as we have mentioned, and constricting it very tightly until the blood is blocked from the upper part of the wound, and over the artery. And the finger should not be removed, but always cool it with very cold water until the blood congeals, thickens, and is severed.
And during this time, one should consider what it needs from a cautery or medicine.
¶ On Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in Wounds, and Similar Matters.
We have already said in the first chapter about every illness in which it is appropriate to use fire for cauterization and burning medicine, and its occasions, causes, instruments, and forms of cauters.
And I have placed the chapters from the top to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter by the same method: so that it may relieve the seeker of what he seeks.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you to learn, O sons, what is in this chapter concerning the fear which is mentioned in the first chapter about cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary to have a more vigorous caution, for in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation with blood often occurs, by which life stands at the opening of the vein, or incision on the abscess, or opening of the fistula, or treatment of the wound, or extraction of the arrow, or incision on the stone, and those similar to these: from all of which trembling and fear are associated, and death often results from most of them.
And I warn you not to let hesitation overcome you, for sometimes methods of people with the methods of illnesses fall upon you in this art. For there are those among them who become angry with their illness, and death is轻于他对他的病情的强烈感受和其恶意的长久。 And when the illness is from fear which signifies death.
And from them is he who gives his ringing sound and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is deadly.
Therefore, you should not agree with him who comes to you from them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your desire and your greed. And do not presume anything from it unless after certain knowledge, which verifies with you that to which a praiseworthy succession arrives.
And use it in the treatment of all your ailments, by prognosis and indication of that to which health appropriately leads.
For in that is your assistance for gaining fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you with His understanding, O sons, and not deprive you of guidance and inspiration: for that is in His hand.

Regarding the treatment of water that accumulates in the heads of children.
Chapter I.
Know that this ailment often occurs in children at birth: or when the midwife grasps the child's head without gentleness.
And it sometimes happens also from a hidden cause which is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this disease in anyone other than children: and death came quickly to all of them whom I saw. Therefore, it seemed to me to abandon the treatment of this condition.
And indeed I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and the head swelled every day until he could not sit up because of the size of his head, and the swelling increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed gathers either between the skin and the bone, or it gathers beneath the bone on the surface.
And the operation for this, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you should make one cut in the middle of the head in width: and the length of the cut should be about two finger widths, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the form of the spatula.
If the moisture has increased more and the abscess is larger, then make two cuts in this form.
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, and the sign of this is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides: and the water is depressed when you press it with your hand towards the interior, and this is not hidden from you: then you must make three cuts in the middle of the head in this manner.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the wounds with cloths and poultices. Then fumigate it with wine and oil from above until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with tents, and ointments.
And do not let the head be bound with equality. And feed the weak with all dry food of little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to determine where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of fluid appear.
For sometimes it is more on the occiput, or on the forehead, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore, make an incision where the abscess and the accumulation of fluid appear to you: and cut it open as much as possible for you.
And be careful not to cut the artery, and let there not be a flow of blood, and the patient does not die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Regarding the incision of the arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs affect the eyes or the chest of someone, and it becomes chronic, and the treatment with medicines does not help: then the final remedy for that is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you want to incise them, the patient should shave their head. Then rub the area with a coarse cloth so that the arteries may appear. Then the patient should bind his neck with any cloth he has. Then observe where the vein pulses. And the locations of both are the depressed areas behind the ears: and it is rarely concealed, except in some individuals.
Then mark with cautery on both sides: and cut each one with a scalpel on both sides sharply, the incision reaching the bone: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it higher up with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers.
For when the vein is cut, blood exits with a pulsating flow, which leaps into the interior frequently.
If the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a distance of three fingers. Then mark with cautery, and cut until you reach the bone.
And what is necessary to let out of the blood is six ounces, and you may emit more or less from it. All of this according to the amount that appears to you from the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
Then attend to the wound. Then if anything remains attached to the bone from the incision, then cut it off, so that a warm abscess does not occur: then bind the wound with strips of linen cloth.
Then care for it with an ointment until it heals.
On the removal of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When an individual suffers from chronic hemicrania or acute catarrhs due to the sharpness of humidities and heat in the temporal arteries, or severe ancient sodas and similar conditions: and if treatment with medical cures does not help: then we have found in these illnesses the extraction of arteries from the temples or their cauterization, as we have narrated.
And the method of the operation for their extraction is to shave the hairs that are on the temples and focus on the visible artery in the temple. For it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is rarely hidden except in a few individuals or due to extreme cold.
If it is hidden from you, then let the patient bind his head with any cloth: then rub the area with a coarse cloth. Or steam the area with hot water until it appears to you with a clear manifestation. Then take the spatula which I shaped a little while ago. Then scrape the skin with it easily until you reach the artery. Then throw the hook into it, and pull it upwards until you extract it from the skin, and make it come out of the tissues beneath it on all sides.
If however the artery is thin: then wrap it with the tip of the hook. Then cut from it the portion according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened, and drawn together, so that bleeding does not occur. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not fully stop.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood, down to three.
If the artery is large: then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a double, strong thread, of silk; or of the gut of a hen, so that corruption does not set in too quickly before the wound heals, and bleeding occurs.
Then cut the excess that is between the two ligaments. Do this at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a knife cautery up to the mouth: until its ends are cut and it remains in place for the same procedure.
And it is better work, unless the patient has a fever, or has a heated temperament. For cautery is one of those things that helps to stop moist discharges. Therefore, it is better treatment.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place should be filled with old lint: and properly made poultices should be placed on top.
And after the dissolution, it should be treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth, and with lint, until it is healed.
If during the operation a flow of blood occurs from an artery, then quickly proceed to sever it with a cautery, or fill the place with zegi: and hold your hand until the blood is severed. If none of those are available to you, then place your finger on the spot until the blood凝固。 And pierce the area with water of intense coldness until the sharpness subsides and constricts as necessary.
And because it is lighter and easier to extract from the artery, you should cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives: let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to almadatem. However, they are much less sharp than the two knives. For if they are sharp like the two knives, cold affects both quickly: and they do not cut the flesh swiftly. Therefore, because there is a little thickness in them, the heat of fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this method, the work is better than any other, lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with cautery: and place both extremely hot until they reach the opening, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between its ends is extended. For they do not coalesce completely with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the treatment of sharp and constant tears from the eyes.
When the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins which are on the outer part of the skull: and no remedy with medicines can close them, and you see the face of the patient already reddened, and he feels a crawling sensation in his forehead, like the crawling of ants: and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their eyelashes are already corroded by corrosion: and his eyelids are heated by the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients required this operation for their treatment, and named it the cure with the Sword.
And it is that you instruct the patient to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equidistant cuts on the forehead according to the length of the forehead: and let the length of the cut be about two fingers, and one cut is equidistant in the middle of the nose, and the second along the length slightly from the movement of the eyelids, and the third from the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries which are on both sides: and keep your hand away from the connection of the two jaws, and let the space of all the cuts be the amount of three fingers joined: and have a piece of prepared sponge ready, or many dried cloths with which to dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which the following is the form. From the incision which is on the temple side, to the second incision: and scrape with it all the skin that is between the two incisions, with a knife, which is above the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a sharp little knife, blunt on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with skin, and its blunt side towards the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues until it reaches what appears of the skin.
Then make it in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after a equal quantity runs out of blood, press the places from the clotted blood pieces.
Then place on every fissure lint from old cloth, and put on them small pieces imbued in wine, oil, or vinegar. And see that a warm abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer a large infusion with warm water. Then care for it with basilicon ointment, and the other things with which wounds are treated: until it is healed.
Chapter Five.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs to the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs happen to someone, and you see their eyes weakened, small, and already the sight of them is weakened: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out from them, and there is a sharp, painful pain deep in the head, followed by sneezing: then consider these occurrences, that those materials and catarrhs do not come unless from many veins.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is that you instruct the patient to rub their forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or another shortly across its width, and begin with an incision from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision go up to the bone: and keep your hand away from the jaw temples, which move during mastication: until when the bone is exposed: and dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the wound with an old catgut, or with linen threads. Then bind it with compresses: and soak the compresses with wine and oil, so that a hot swelling does not occur.
And when you loosen it, and see that the hot swelling has already diminished: then you must rub the area, until new flesh begins to grow.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth: such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony: and prepare an ointment from them, to be administered for the generation of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter Six.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is of one of four types: either a mineral stone, or similar to stone, such as iron and glass: or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans: or it is a fluid substance, such as water, vinegar, and things like them: or some animal.
When a stone or stone-like substance that does not add to or grow in the ear falls into it, expose the ear to the sun: if you see the stone, then drip something from violet oil or hyacinth into the ear. Then administer its extraction and make the person sneeze with condysis, and plug the nostrils as the sneeze comes, after you place pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upward.
For often it happens with this care.
If it does not come out, then administer its extraction with tweezers, that is, with fine pincers: whose form is this.
If it comes out with tweezers, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a fine, few-fold blind hook.
If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of metal, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole deeply, and pack what is around the tube with wax softened with oil, so that there is no way for air except through the tube: then extract with your strong inhalation through the tube.
For often it comes out with what we have mentioned. If it does not come out this way, then take a small amount of birdlime or glue, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are caught, and place it on the end of a rod after you wrap a cloth properly around it; then insert it into the ear hole easily after you dry the ear from moisture.
If it does not come out with all that we have mentioned, then hurry to incise before a warm abscess or spasms occur.
And the method of the incision is to bleed the patient first in the cephalic vein: and let blood from him according to the measure of his strength. Then have the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its fat in a depressed area: and let the incision be of a crescent shape until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever instrument is possible for you.
Then sew the wound by your hour with speed, and care for it until it heals.
If a thing falling into the ear is of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its removal.
If however it does not obey you to come out, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this shape.
And administer with it the cutting of that kind from the grains that fall into the ear, and do not do this when you think that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you place it in many pieces: then extract them with hooks, or with a fine thread, or with suction, as we have said, for its extraction is alleviated.
In water that enters the ear, the patient must use sneezing with condysi at first: and already fill his ear with some old cooked substance: and he lies on that ear in which there is water. If it exits through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, soft, and heat them with fire for a moment, and let the patient insert one of them into the opening of his ear: and jump on one foot from that side, and strike with another stone on the stone which is in the ear. Therefore, he should not stop doing this with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water exits so that you take one from a parpyro or from a reed, and insert one end of it into the ear: and light the other end with fire until most of it is burned. Then insert another rush tube, and do this many times until all the water comes out: and draw it out with the cannula as was mentioned previously in the stone.
In the extraction of an animal entering it, if it is of a small body, like a flea, and similar, care for it as I said in the division.
But if it is of a large body, which appears to the senses: then administer its extraction with hooks and forceps: and its removal is easier than all those held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division, and in the treatment of distillations, and your treatment does not help: then you must look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something of the worms appears there, then extract it with hooks or with subtle forceps.
If however nothing appears there, then take a cannula, which has this form, narrow below, wide above: and insert the slender end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate it: then suck with a strong suction. Do this many times until all the worms come out.
If however they do not obey you to come out, then seal around the cannula with wax, as I said in the stone.
If they do not come out with what we have said: then administer the distillations which the ancients have already used to destroy worms: and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring with which you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, wide above.
Let it be such that the plunger which is in the concavity of the tube is properly made of bronze. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap cotton at its end with a proper wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or what you wish of these medicines into the tube: and let it be in the ear; then insert the rod with the cotton above, and press it with your hand in a moderate compression until the oil is pushed into the auditory cavity, and the patient feels it internally; and what is infused into the ear should already be warmed slightly with fire: and beware that what is distilled into it is not very cold; for the medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
Regarding the treatment of ailments affecting the ear.
Sometimes infants come out of their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it has also happened to some people that their ear canal has become obstructed due to an injury or flesh grown in it.
And this obstruction sometimes occurs in the deep part of the ear canal, which is not visible: and it sometimes affects the outer part of the canal slightly, which is visible.
Therefore, that which occurs in the deep part of the ear and which is not visible, is in most cases difficult to heal.
And in that which follows the sight, you must place the ear of the patient in the sun, and look at it: then if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a subtle spatula, whose form is as follows, its end should have some small width, part of which should be sharp: and the rest of the spatula should be of two smooth sides: so that it does not hurt the ear.
If therefore the obstruction is flesh grown in the ear canal: then hold it with a subtle hook, and cut it with great ease until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is in the depth of the ear: then take a slender, smooth probe, and heat it slightly with fire: then insert it into the ear canal.
If therefore you feel the obstruction with it, which the patient finds with heaviness of hearing, attempt to open it with ease: and beware lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a lint according to the size of the opening which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days until you are sure of the wound's healing.
Or take lint, and soak it in rose water, and sprinkle ground zedoary on it, and use it.
If you see the ear already forming an abscess: then you must remove the lint, and allow it to be with another piece of lint which you wrap in cerote made with rose oil until the warm abscess settles: then care for it until it heals.
If however a flow of blood occurs then submerge a sponge or cloths in cold water and place them on the ear and use the remaining treatment which stops the aforementioned flow of blood in the chapter on distillations.
On the treatment of eyelid inflammation.
Chapter VIII.
These eyelid inflammations that occur in the eyelids are sometimes moist and are sometimes dry.
Therefore, you must hold them with forceps or with a hook, and cut them out with a spatula from their roots. Then place ground zegi on the affected areas.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lentil-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
On the treatment of eyelid tumors.
Chapter IX.
Often something occurs in the eyelids that resembles granulomas in their strength and hardness, and because of this it is called Granum: and it is an aggregation of coarse humidity in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
The operation therefore is to consider then if there is a stone coming out of it, which appears from the eyelid, and they move to all parts, and its treatment is easy.
Find therefore on it a fissure across the width. Then remove the outer layer from all sides with ease until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
If however the cutting off of it is not prepared for you unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a slight incision, then it harms the patient somewhat.
If the fissure is large, then join it with stitching and care for it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For the ointment restores it and causes flesh to grow over it.
And if the stone is inclined towards the inside of the ear towards the inner surface, then turn the eyelid inside out, and suspend the stone with a hook; besides which you may need an incision, and press it from all sides.
If the eyelid is extended by an incision, it does not harm in any way.
Then wash the eye after the stone is cut out with salty water: and treat the area with something that causes flesh to grow over it until the patient heals.
Regarding the treatment of AXIRNATH of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is the fat that forms in the lining of the upper eyelids: and this happens most often to children; and it weighs down their eyes, and they get catarrhs in the mornings, and they cannot look at sunlight because tears rush to them, and you see them always lying on their faces and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, which inflation has now covered: and indeed the prominence of Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of the operation is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare some wax on it, and make a circle out of that wax according to the size that contains Axirnath on all sides. Then place it on it, and press with your fingers on all sides so that Axirnath gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with a scalpel making incisions through its width, and let not the incision be deeper than that which is made in bloodletting.
Or it is necessary to cut through the entire skin until you reach Axirnath, for in many cases Axirnath protrudes from the incision in the shape of a piece of white fat.
Extract it with a cloth that you have inserted between your fingers to the outside: and you turn your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed: and be careful not to insert anything into the incision so that you do not reach the eye with the scalpel and injure it.
If Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, you should add a little more easily into the fissure until Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who rub salt and place it in the concavity of the fissure, so that what remains of the moisture may melt.
Then care for it until it heals.
If, however, a warm abscess occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
Regarding the conditions of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid softens, causing it to droop more than it should.
Chapter XI.
When additional hairs grow on the eyelids beyond the natural course and become old: then they harm the eye and cause various illnesses to occur, such as continuous tearing, softening of the eyelids, and opacification, until it becomes a cause for the destruction of the eye.
And indeed, Ataxmir of the eye is treated in four ways. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with sharp medicine, according to what was mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterization. It is treated by cutting and stitching. Or with tubes, as I say.
You must place the head of the patient in your lap. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert the needle into it, where there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with the probe.
Then find on the inner part of the eyelid beneath the added hairs with the spatula applied from the larger tear duct to the smaller one.
Then remove the thread, and place a small piece of cloth or linen under the eyelid: then mark on the eyelid with wax, which should be like the figure of a myrtle leaf.
However, it must be done according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: for it now varies among people.
For there are those who need a large portion to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount that is relaxed from the eyelid.
Then with the spatula find above the two lines you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller one, and let the incision be near the natural hairs, as thick as the probe.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape the entire area. Then sew together the two lips with a fine thread of wool using a needle: and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what remains of the threads above the two eyebrows, with some adhesive substances, if you wish to do so: and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture and threads for three days, or four: afterwards care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture, and care for it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing of the wound and its joining: but it will be better in the future.
The ancients taught this method of treatment.
However, there is careful attention to the patient, and it is from good practice, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is also in Ataxmir: and it is to mark on the eyelid a figure that resembles the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have mentioned.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate or joined hooks, according to this following shape, then cut away what is excess with small scissors, according to this shape, with equal cuts.
If it is not possible for you to hold the hook and it does not align: then take a needle with thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert one thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct: and bring the threads together between your fingers with equality.
Then lift your hand with equal elevation: and lift the skin over which the marked seam is, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and care for it until it heals.
If it happens that a swelling occurs near your incision or your cut: relieve that swelling with cerote and similar substances from soothing ointments.
And it sometimes happens to the eyelids that their hairs turn inward.
Therefore, you should use what we have said about the incision on it: but suturing both is more skilled. Regarding the treatment of ataxmia with reed tubes.
And it is such that you turn the eyelid and split the fissure which is inside, according to what I have narrated. Then skillfully make two reed tubes or two fine sticks: the length of which should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of a spatula: and you will now incise at the ends of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully bring each one over the excess of the eyelid, and tightly bind the two reed tubes on both sides with a strong tie, and leave it for several days. For skin that is tightly bound will die, and turn black, and become putrid, until it falls off by itself.
If it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
When therefore it solidifies, lift the eyelid: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two cans, which you will make according to this figure, and the quantity in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On the removal of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation is such that you take a fine needle and insert into it a thread made of fine, soft silk. Then bring together the ends of it and tie each end with a very fine knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then arrange another fine thread under it briefly, and knot the ends of it if you wish.
Then let the patient place his head in your lap, and be near the sun so that the operation may be clearly seen: for it is a fine or minute operation.
Then insert the needle with Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you draw it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up to above the Alinxotati: and insert into the Alinxotati that hair: whether it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair comes out with the Alinxotati from the eyelid along with the natural hair.
If the eyelash is pulled and the hair does not come out with it: pull the eyelash downward, using the thread you made for it, until the eyelash comes out from the upper opening, and you can see it with your eye: then pull the eyelash and the thread: extract both, and close the eye: leave it closed for two days or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either attach a long hair from natural hairs to it, and lift with it: or leave it until it grows longer over time, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati occurs in the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental Alxatrati occurs from the healing of wounds, or cuts, or cauterizations, and similar procedures.
And the method of treatment is to split that healing, and to separate its edges, and place a strip of linen between them, and bind it until it heals.
And you should not use drying and constricting substances in its treatment: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather use things that soften it, such as Greek hay: and foment with water in which marshmallow and flaxseed have been cooked: and anoint with liquid dyaquilon mixed with some oil: and wrap it with a linen cloth, and treat it thus.
And the treasure of your care is that you endeavor with all your ingenuity that it not become consolidated, according to the form which was at first.
And this operation which we have mentioned is merely a correction of Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not return entirely to what it was.
Regarding the treatment of Alxatrati which is performed on the lower eyelid, and it is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This condition which occurs below is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one occurs from a wound, or a tear, or a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of the operation is to take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, making it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread comes out at both ends of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upward with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. If the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take the awl, and place it in the tear, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two tears on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two tears be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and form an angle: until when brought together they form this shape, which is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount which is its sharp lower side, from what follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts: sew it with wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said about the softening medicines and plasters until they heal.
And if an Alxatrati occurs due to a cut, suture, or cautery: then it is necessary to make a flat incision under the eyelash hairs, according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a plaster as we have said.
And the sum of the discourse on the treatment of Alxatrati, whether it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed accordingly as it is prepared according to the form of Alxatrati.
For it is indeed of many diversities in form: and the prudent artisan prepares with whatever manner is possible to him until he restores the figure to its natural form or near it, and the patient himself according to what is suitable to him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Regarding the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation occurs in many people so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the white of the eye and prevents it from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, you must introduce the tip of a probe under the eyelid and lift it up; or extend it with a hook; then cut the adherence with a delicate spatula that is not sharp like a cutting spatula, but rather slightly blunt: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula does not harm the eye with its sharpness; and let your incision be such that you pare away that adherence easily until when the eyelid returns to its natural form and the entire adherence heals.
Then pour salty water into the eye, wash it with water: or distill onto it from a sieve made from dissolved zinc and water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with flax oil: and place wool soaked in egg white over the eye: and after the third day use a sieve, and with plasters, until it heals.
Regarding the incision of the eyelid and the protruding flesh of the tears.
Chapter Sixteen.
You should know that the eyelid can be cut in two ways. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to a hard, delicate sieve. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which when touched with a iron, or you try to take it with a hook, breaks apart, and the hook does not hold onto it.
And both types do not begin unless from the larger tear duct, extending gradually until it covers the sight and obstructs the light and movement of the eye.
And the method of operating in its incision is: that the patient places his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then gather the eyelid with a hook of few twists, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the horse, ox, or cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle for a moment, and fix it in the middle of the eyelid: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the eyelid with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scrape with the hair the side of the eyelid that follows the pupil, as if you were sewing it with the hair to its end.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that the continuous flow of tears is not impeded by its incision.
And sometimes the claw is extended with a hook alone: or with a thread, as we have said. Then it should be scraped with a gentle spatula, whose form is as follows.
And beware that the spatula does not touch the horny membrane, and that no rupture occurs in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the incision, throw some ground salt into the eye, or something made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then care for it with what is necessary until it heals.
If the claw is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor can a hook be fixed in it, then this kind of claw is often treated with medications and healed as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the patient's eye, and take a fine, gentle spatula of mild sharpness, according to this form. And scrape the claw gently from above with subtlety. If you see that it becomes稀薄和破裂，并且刻刀在其上留下印记，那么用你的时间向眼睛滴入由ziniar制成的细丝，或红色细丝，或磨碎的盐：然后封住眼睛直到第二天。
Then again on the same operation, until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a warm aposteme contrary to you in the eye: then let it be, and treat the aposteme until it heals.
Then perform the operation on it reasonably, until it heals.
However, if the prominence of the lacrimal flesh harms the eye greatly, it should be suspended with a hook, and a part of it should be cut off: and do not make the incision too deep, so that the course of tears is not affected.
Then apply a red salve to the lacrimal area, or one made from zinc, until it heals.
Regarding the incision of Alguardenic, and the flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in the eyes of some people, red flesh is formed or positioned over them until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or presses on the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a damask rose.
And the operation on it is such that the patient should place their head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks: and hold it with one forceps or tweezers.
Then cut the flesh initially with a scalpel until the entire removal is completed. And be careful with the eye, so that you do not harm it during the operation: and let your cut with which you cut it be, either with a spatula, which we mentioned in the cutting of the hoof: or with small scissors, with which basil is gathered, according to how its form will come after this chapter.
When therefore your cut is completed and all thealguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with ground salt: or sprinkle on it red powder and similar things from corrosive medicines: and place over the eye outside a cloth with egg white: so that it is secured from a warm abscess.
If any of the alguardenici remains and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a warm abscess: then abandon the eye and treat it with that which soothes the abscess.
Then repeat the procedure on it until it heals.
And similarly treat the added flesh that occurs in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary to avoid the treatment of any weak eye by natural means or by accidental means, using iron: and do not involve your hand in any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate that operation.
Regarding the removal of sebel from the eye.
Chapter Eighteen.
Sebel are red veins that form over the eye and prevent it from functioning properly: and they weaken the eye over time.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye in which there is sebel is strong and has no other illness besides the sebel: then remove the sebel.
And it is that you instruct the patient to place his head in your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your discretion: and let the hook be of subtle double bend, according to this form, or let there be two hooks set stepwise in one body, according to this form.
Then collect with fine forceps those veins with subtlety, and cleanse the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and they dissolve: and beware lest you injure the eye with the ends of the spatula.
And let your operation be at noon in the presence of the sun, and be firm in your operation very much: so that you cut only those veins.
And when you have completed, then distill into the eye green sap or red: so that by its sharpness it corrodes what remains of the sebel.
If however it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then apply a plaster to the eye with something that alleviates the pain of the eye: and leave it for several days, until its pain subsides: and it shall be secured from a warm abscess: then proceed on it according to the same method, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
Regarding the restoration of Arissati to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians name Arissati the Fistula, which you treat with a cautery or with an acute burning medicine, according to what was previously described.
And if it is not healed, then there is no skill in it: unless you incise above the abscess at its origin, and extract all the moisture and pus within it until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument whose form is this.
And it is named Asperi capitis.
Let it be made of Indian iron, and let its head be round like an awl, which should be pointed with the fineness of a file or with a subtle prick of an aliskilfer: and place it on the corrupted part of the bone.
Then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand for a moment until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the area with drying and astringent medicines.
If therefore the area is consolidated and new flesh grows there, and the course of pus is stopped: and it remains for forty days without returning, without abscessing, and no accident occurs in it: then you know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no skill in it, unless you redirect the fistula to the nasal aperture according to this form.
And it is such that you expose the second bone with iron or a sharp medicine: and when the bone is exposed, then take a drill according to this form, let the iron end be triangular: and its wood should be a tapered pyramid down to the tip, as you see.
Then place it over the mouth itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and move your hand a little away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the drill until it penetrates the bone, and let the patient feel it; by which he finds air coming out of it when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh is born, and the place hardens: then the material which was flowing outward returns to the nose: thus it is a milder condition for the patient.
Regarding the reduction of a protrusion of the eye.
Chapter Twenty.
When the eye protrudes entirely, and no harm or reduction occurs in vision: then you must comfort the patient: then bleed him: afterwards place cupping on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the eye an ointment made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and gum Arabic. Then bind the eye over the poultice with many bandages.
And the method of the bandages is: take soft cloths, many of them, and fold each cloth into four: make many of them: then place them one on top of another according to the amount needed by the eye: then bind them tightly with a strong binding using a hard cloth.
And again with suction without scarification.
Then remove the suction, and leave the eye bandaged day and night.
If however the bandage softens again, then it must be tightened greatly: then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has returned to the grape skin, it is good: and if not, then again apply the plaster, the powder, the bandage, and the suction, until it heals.
Concerning the incision of the grape skin.
Chapter Twenty-One.
When a rupture occurs in the grape skin, then it protrudes and extends beyond the eyelids like a grape seed, and the appearance of the person is marred because of this, and you wish to cut it: then the procedure for it is, as I narrate: and it is to insert a needle at the root of the grape from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle with double thread from the lacrimal part, and make it penetrate: and leave the first needle in its position.
Then cut the double thread, and tie it to the grape part around the needle: and tighten it greatly.
Then remove the needle, and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye: and leave it until the thread falls off with the grape.
Then treat the eye with what strengthens it until the wound heals.
And sometimes the eye is bound all around with a circle of cloth until it gains great prominence.
Then pierce it with a fine spatula that reaches the depth of the eye, and let the whitish moisture flow out, and the eye descends, and immediately proceed: then bind it until it heals.
Regarding the treatment of Alcomnati.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
This illness which is called Alcomnati is not anything but a pus that gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is to have the patient sit on a prepared seat or stand straight.
Then take his head in your hands from two sides and move it until you see the pus come to the lower parts of your eyes. Then it should be fixed, and not allowed to descend, and the light should be restored: and the patient should see things as he saw before: and if it does not go down further, you know that it is water.
If the pus does not prepare to descend with what we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula and use it to pierce slightly above the corneal membrane at the junction of the conjunctiva with the corneal layer in the crown, until the pus comes out.
When it comes out, then drip warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled Greek hay and honey: then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
On the treatment of water descending into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already spoken about the types of water in the division and what is suitable for piercing, declaring and explaining it, then take that same thing according to health.
Then it is necessary to have the patient sit between your hands, facing the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye tightly, and press it strongly: then with your right hand if the eye containing water is the left eye, or with your left hand if it is the right eye, hold his eyelid.
Then take with your right hand the probe if the eye is the left: or with your left hand if the eye is the right.
Then place the tip of the probe near the cornea at the thickness of the radius where the white of the eye is visible near the smaller lacrimal: then push the probe with force: and you turn your hand with it until it penetrates into the sclera of the eye: and you will feel that the probe has now reached an empty space.
And it is necessary that the measurement of the probe's journey to the depth be the distance from the pupil to the end of the black part: and this is the cornea of the eye. For you will see it in the pupil of the eye itself due to the clarity of the corneal membrane.
Then place the probe until it reaches the location where the water is. Then press it below once after another.
If therefore water descends immediately, then the patient sees what he opens his vision to immediately, and the eyelid in his eye.
Then rest for a while. If therefore water descends, then remove it secondly, except for the eyelid to exit.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not rise: then remove the eyelid with ease: and you turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve something from clear gem salt in water: and wash the eye inside with it. Then place outside the eye the entire piece of cotton, or wool soaked in rose oil, and goose grease: and bandage the healthy eye with it.
We, and those of our time, place ground cinnamon with egg white on it.
If the eyelid does not obey you for entering into the eye because of its hardness, since it is from a person whose eye is very hard: then you must take a spatula, which is called Alberid, whose form is this.
Therefore, insert with it only the connector, and do not put your hand deeply into the hole: and you do this only to make a way to the subtle place of the eyelid.
Then insert the eyelid as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, let a properly made sleeping place be prepared for the patient, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room: and let him be prohibited from all movements, and from coughing: and give him food that suits his nature: and let him not move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the binding be according to its arrangement up to the third day.
Then unbind him in that dark room, and test his vision: and make him see things. Then bind it again until the seventh day.
And it should not be done during the time of healing, or immediately after the incision: rather, it should be avoided because water ascends quickly through a strong gaze.
If a warm abscess occurs, then it is necessary to unbind the eye before the seventh day: and treat it with what calms the abscess until it is calmed: then restore its sight, and place over his face a covering to regulate his vision under it for several days, while he remains in that dark room.
Then let him gradually come out of the house and move about in his own chambers.
And know that in zaltea the student is not excused from seeing several times: then he may dare to perform the operation.
And indeed it has come to our attention from some who are from Alayrach, who said that in Alayrach a pipe was made through which water is drawn.
Therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from wax, so that one who is ignorant may stand over them.
Let them be made from proper bronze: and let their ends be shaped with the same fineness into triangles, and let the end be sharp.
Chapter XXIV.
On the care of flesh growing in the nose.
Sometimes diverse kinds of flesh grow in the nose, some of which resemble multi-footed scorpions: and from these is a cancerous, stony, brownish flesh: and there is also one which is soft flesh, not brownish in color.
In those among these flesh growths that are soft, not deceptive, nor cancerous, you should have the patient sit between your hands facing the sun, and open his nostrils, and cast a hook into those flesh growths: then pull them outwards, and cut what you grasp of them with a thin sharp spatula from one side, until you know that all the flesh is now removed.
If any part remains that cannot be cut off: then scrape it with one of the fine instruments easily, until nothing of it remains. If blood dominates you, or a hot abscess occurs: then apply what is appropriate to it: or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then quickly cauterize it until the blood is severed and all the flesh is removed.
Then after the cut vinegar, and water, or wine is thrown into the nose. If the nose is open and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then you know that he is already healed.
If the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that flesh has grown within the upper nasal bones to which the instrument does not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary to introduce a linen thread which has some thickness and many knots in it: place between each knot the length of a finger, or less: the patient should be instructed to insert one end of the thread into his nose with a tube, or whatever is possible for him after he makes it similar to azer: and he should draw it inward until it reaches the cavity, and exit above his throat.
And indeed, boys in schools often do something similar to this: and it is easy for one who wishes to do so.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out above the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then the flesh with the knots on the thread should be carefully cut. Do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then withdraw the thread: and place in the nose after wiping away the blood a licinium that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then place at the end of the thing in the nose a tube of lead for several days until it heals.
If however it requires treatment which dries, use that.
To make a head purgation device, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it out of silver or bronze: so that it is similar to a small lamp, open like a seashell: and its spout similarly.
And if you wish to make a closed tube, like a cane.
And the shell of the instrument for making head purgation should be full, open: to which there is a handle at the end of it, with which you hold it when you heat oil or what you wish from juices and liquid substances in it.
Chapter XXV.
Often a polyp grows at the end of the nose, and it enlarges and increases with days until its appearance becomes ugly.
And because of this, it is necessary to cut it down at the beginning of its appearance and root out all of it. Then place on the area either a cautery or a burning medicine that serves in its place.
If therefore the cutting is delayed until it enlarges, then consider if it is stony, hard, of a dark color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron: for it is a cancerous growth.
For I have often seen that when an abscess is cut open in a person, great harm results from it for the one who had it.
And if the abscess has a soft touch, is not of a dark color, and you see that it can be completely incised, then evacuate the diseased matter, and cut it without fear, without dread: and treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
About sewing the nose, the lip, and the ear.
Know that when there is a disruption of continuity in one of these cartilages, the operation is of little benefit, except in certain individuals.
Therefore, when something like that happens to someone, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound with its own blood: then bring together the edges of the wound with sutures: then care for it until it heals.
And if the edges of the disrupted continuity are already separated and each part has become healed: then you must remove all the skin from its visible surface until it bleeds. Then bring together the two edges with sutures and bind them tightly, and dust over it dragon's blood and powdered frankincense: and place a piece of diaphoretic plaster over the powders, and another consolidating plaster: and leave it bound for two or three days. Then loosen it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads fall off by themselves. Then care for it with an ointment until it heals.
The method of sewing is to join the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have mentioned in the stitching of the abdomen; or join the separation with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the removal of lip knots.
Many people suffer from hard swellings similar to grains of a herb, mostly on the inner parts of their lips: some of them are smaller, and others are larger.
Therefore, you must turn the lip inside out and cut above the entire knot, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it from all sides.
Then fill the area after the incision with ground zegi until the bleeding stops.
Then it should be treated with vinegar, and the area where there is astringency should be cared for until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the incision of the flesh growth above the gum.
Often, additional flesh grows above the gum, which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore, you must suspend it with a hook: or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and allow the pus to flow, or the blood.
Then place ground zegi or other astringent, drying powders on the area.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does return, then cut it a second time and cauterize it; for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
About the reason for teeth with iron.
Sometimes on the surfaces of teeth, from the inner and outer part, and between the gums, rough, ugly crusts gather, and they sometimes blacken and turn yellow and green until corruption from that reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul because of it.
Therefore, you must make the patient sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the mill and the tooth where the crusts appear, until nothing of them remains, and do likewise with the blackness, greenness, and yellowness, and others until they are cleaned.
If what is in them from the beginning of the scraping is removed, it is good: and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second, and a third, until finally your effort achieves what you wish.
And you should know that a tooth requires scrapers of various forms and many shapes: according to how they are prepared for your operation: therefore, the scraper with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of scrapers should be prepared and available to you, as you see.
Chapter XXX.
About the extraction of tooth pain.
You must cure the tooth of its pain with all ingenuity and delay in its extraction. For there is nothing to replace it when it is extracted, since it is a substance of high value. Therefore, when there is no excuse for its extraction, then it must be extracted when the patient is compelled, so that it may be stabilized and you may be certain about the painful tooth.
For often the pain deceives the patient, and they think it is in a sound tooth and extract it; then the pain does not subside until the diseased tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen this from the practice of bloodletters.
When therefore the painful tooth is confirmed to you, then you must scarify around the tooth with a spatula that has some efficacy until the gum is loosened on all sides; then move it with your fingers or with delicate forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps firmly on it, and hold the head of the patient between your knees; once you have a good grip so that it does not move, then pull the tooth straight out so that you do not break it.
If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it under the tooth on all sides with ease, and attempt to move it as you did at first.
If the tooth is perforated or corroded, then you must fill that hole with cloth and grasp it with the tip of a slender probe so that it does not break when you clamp it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you perform the scarification around the gum meticulously from all sides greatly.
And guard your strength so that you do not break it, and let part of it remain, and let it return upon the sick from that恶意which is greater than his first pain.
And beware lest you do what ignorant bloodletters do in their haste and boldness regarding the removal of it, besides administering what we have narrated.
For they often cause great illnesses to occur in people, the healing of which is more difficult, and all its roots remain or some of them; or they extract it along with some bones of the jaw: as we have testified many times.
Then it should be treated after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the places (for it often happens), then grind something from spurge, and fill the area with it: and if not, cauterize it if spurge is not available to you.
The forms of delicate forceps with which the tooth is moved should have long ends, a short handle, and be thick so that they do not double when pressed against the tooth: the handles should be thick as you see, so that when you grip them, they do not slip or bend: the ends should be short, and made of Indian iron or well-made steel, with straight ends: and at their ends there should be teeth, some of which enter and grip firmly with a proper constriction.
And sometimes the ends are made in the shape of the mouth of a bird called a stork, and they have a strong grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
Regarding the removal of teeth and the extraction of fractured jawbones.
When something remains attached to the tooth that is being extracted, then it is necessary to place goat's hair with butter on the area for one day or two days until the area softens; then insert algasti or forceps, whose ends resemble the mouth of a bird called stork. This is the form of the forceps.
Let their ends be made like a file inside: or like aliskilfegi.
If it does not obey and come out with these forceps, then you must make a cavity above the root, and expose all the flesh, and insert an instrument called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let the end be short and somewhat thick: and let it not be hollow, so that it does not break.
If the root comes out with this, it is good: and if not, assistance must be given with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first triangular end, which has some thickness.
The form of the slender triangular end.
And with other instruments and tools which have been mentioned previously in the discussion of teeth.
Assistance is also given with this instrument which resembles a large hook, of this form, with a twisted triangular end, which has some thickness so that it does not break: and let it not be hollow.
And you shall know that the instruments of teeth are many: and likewise the other instruments are not counted, and the wise craftsman, subtle in his art, finds the instrument by himself according to what the operation and the illnesses themselves signify.
For there are bitternesses to which the ancients did not speak of an instrument because of the diversity of their kinds.
If however the bone is fractured from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or it putrefies: then examine it in its place with what is suitable to it from the instruments and forceps which I have mentioned in the extraction of roots, and assist with a lever, whose form is this.
Let there be in it something of gradually increasing thickness, so that the bone may be grasped or held firmly, and not slip away, until the bone comes out: and restore the place with medicines appropriate for it.
If however there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then scrape it from its putrefaction, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter XXXII.
On the filing of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, the appearance is marred by this: and especially when this happens in women and girls, that is, in public women.
Therefore, you should consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and does not fit, or its filing or smoothing is not possible: then extract it. And if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is this.
It is similar to a small beak and is made of hardened iron with a very sharp end.
And let your cut be where you cut it over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another of the teeth is not moved.
If it is prominent and requires filing: then file it with an iron file, whose form is this.
Let it be entirely of iron, and its handle of strong small points, like the file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved and falls out: then smooth it, at the end scrape it with some razors.
If there is a tooth from which a part is broken and it injures the tongue: then it must be filed until the roughness of that break is removed, and it is leveled: and flattened, so that it does not injure the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
Regarding the rotation of teeth with gold threads.
When the front teeth are shaken and jarred by a blow or fall: and the patient cannot bite down on what he eats so it does not fall out: and you have treated them with astringent medicines, but the treatment was ineffective: then the method is to bind them with gold thread: for silver turns green and rots after some days: but gold never changes.
And let the thread be of medium thickness according to the size that fits between the teeth.
And indeed the form of rotation is that you take a thread and insert its double between two healthy teeth: then weave with the two ends of the thread between the moved teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the needle all the way to the healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving all the way back to the part from which you started: and hold your hand with subtlety and wisdom until it is not removed entirely: and let your constriction as you pull the thread be at the roots of the teeth so that it does not escape.
Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors: and bring together both ends, and tie them tightly: and hide them between a healthy tooth and a loose tooth, so that they do not hurt the tongue: then leave it thus tight as long as it remains.
If it loosens or breaks, tie them with another thread, and it will stay that way for the entire time.
And this is the form of teeth and the figure of rotation of two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And it sometimes happens that one or two teeth return to their places after falling out and are retained as we have described and remain: and none but a wise and experienced artisan can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow's bone is adapted: and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in position, whereby the tooth is secured and tightened as we have said: and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from an injury that has already solidified.
And the operation is to open the mouth of the patient in your hand, and lift his tongue: then cut that nervous ligament across until the tongue is freed from its restraint.
If there is something hard and knotted, and it is due to the consolidation of an injury: then apply a hook to it, and cut it across until you sever the ligament and loosen the knot.
And beware that the cut does not go deep into the flesh and sever an artery there, causing a flow of blood.
Then sew up the trace of the incision with rose water, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a strip of linen, which the patient should hold throughout the night: so that it does not solidify again.
If a flow of blood occurs, then place ground zeggium on the site. If the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a mottled cautery that suits it: then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue which resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function: and it may grow so large until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun: and examine the abscess.
If then you see it is of a brown or black color, hard, to which the patient does not feel sensation: then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it inclines to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then apply a hook to it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and free it from all sides. If the blood overpowers you during your operation, then apply ground zegi until the blood coagulates: then return to your operation, until you extract it with its entirety.
Then it should be treated with vinegar and salt: and care for it with the appropriate further treatment until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of abscesses of the two tonsils.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
Sometimes there occur between the throat glands, which resemble almonds that occur externally: and they are called the two tonsils: which when you treat with what we have said in the division, and they do not heal, then examine.
If then it is a hard abscess, of a brown color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing the flow of blood, but rather leave it alone until it ripens. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is of white color, round, and its root is slender: then it must be cut open.
And the procedure is such that before performing the operation, you should consider whether the warm abscess has completely subsided or decreased somewhat: then have the patient sit facing the sun, and let his head be in your lap: open his mouth, and let the servant take between his hands, and press his tongue below with an instrument, which has this form.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which is fine like a knife.
When therefore you press the tongue with it and the abscess is revealed to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can, along with some of the tissue.
Then cut it with an instrument, which has the following form. It resembles scissors: however, its ends should be curved so that the opening of each is directly opposite the other's point: hence, make sure it is made of hardened iron or steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a spatula, which has this form. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision.
Then after the incision, the patient should gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate bark has been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and similar astringents, until the bleeding is stopped: then care for it until it heals.
And at times, abscesses other than the two tonsils develop in the throat. Cut them accordingly as I have said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I have indeed treated a woman with an abscess that had developed in her throat, which was drawing her to darkness, loss of senses, and nearly closed her throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it had already prevented her from eating and drinking water: and she had almost reached death, if it had lasted for one day or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches, extending over the two nasal openings.
I hurried quickly and attached a hook to one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion was drawn out from it.
Then I cut it where it protruded from the nasal opening.
Then I did that which protruded from the other nostril.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue. Then I inserted a hook into the abscess. Then I cut off part of it, and only a little blood flowed out. Therefore, the woman's throat was healed, and she began to drink water immediately, then took some food.
Therefore, I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times over a long period of time: and the abscess succeeded in place of what I was cutting, until it was extended too much and with it that condition.
I therefore devised and cauterized the abscess in the throat.
It therefore stood from the addition: then I walked away, and I did not know what God did with her after me.
Regarding the incision of an abscess on the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula and it becomes abscessed and is long: then it is called perpendicular. And if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division and the treatment does not succeed: and you see that the warm abscess has subsided from it and is thin: then it is necessary to cut it open.
And if what is aggregated from it is round and does not have length, and is of a bloody or brown or black color, and does not have sensation: then it is necessary to avoid cutting it. For in it there is fear over the sick.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner I have described by its whiteness and length, make the sick person sit before the sun: and press his tongue with the instrument of which I previously spoke: then insert the hook in the fold, and pull it down: and cut it with one of the two instruments I mentioned for incising both tonsils.
And you must not cut from it except that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut more from it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use what we have mentioned in the incision of both tonsils: and care for it until it heals.
If the sick person is terrified of the incision: then you must use ingenuity in cauterizing it without fear and worry.
And the method of cauterization for it is not without sharp water: and it is to place the sick person's head in your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument we mentioned. Then take of the water that I told you about in the chapter on cauterizing, and mix it with unextinguished charcoal, and make it not too thick nor too thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, which has this form.
Let the end of it where the medicine is placed be concave, like the cavity of a spoon's bowl.
And place the instrument with medicine on the ulcer itself: and let the patient lie on his side, so that saliva may run from his mouth due to internal medicine, so that nothing of it descends to his throat and injures him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you should apply it to the ulcer for half an hour, until you see it already blackened, and the irritation of the medicine calmed.
And if you want to take a quill, and wrap it around the end of the rod, and pour the medicine into the quill, and insert the rod with the quill into the tube above, until the quill adheres to the ulcer.
Do this many times until you achieve what you wish from the cauterization of the ulcer. Then leave it: for it dried up and fell off after three days, or four.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after cauterization, clean around the ulcer with a quill soaked in butter, and cleanse with it what is around it from the medicine: and mix it with cold water.
And it should be treated externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
Therefore, this method of treatment is more beneficial than incision and farther from fear.
And sometimes the ulcer is treated with something finer than cauterization and incision: it is treated with fumigation, according to this method. Take a reed, and hyssop, and origanum, and rue, and sesame, and chamomile, and southernwood, and similar ones among herbs: and gather all of them, or some of them in a pot, and let them be submerged with vinegar, and boiled: and the pot should be plastered with clay decently: and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot on which a concave instrument is set according to this manner.
Let it be made of silver or bronze: and insert the end with the ball into the mouth of the sick person until the vapor rises to the throat above the tube, until the throat is greatly obscured.
Then apply it repeatedly on it until it dries.
And beware, do not perform this treatment at the beginning of the onset of the abscess. For it usually increases the abscess: and you should not do this unless at the stage when the abscess is declining and becoming cooler.
If this instrument is not available to you, then take a cane, and attach a piece of ox horn to its end so that the mouth of the sick person is not burned: for the ox horn prevents the vapor from burning the mouth: and this is a good treatment that ensures safety.
Regarding the extraction of spines and bones from the throat.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
Often a fish bone or spine, or something else, adheres to the throat.
