Then let them be kept day and night in sweet water until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their stomachs. Then the body should be purged first with bloodletting and with cupping. Then clean the patient's limb until it turns red. Then place them on it.
Therefore, when they are filled and fall off, and it is possible to suction the area with a cupping vessel, then it is most beneficial for treatment: and if not, then wash the area with vinegar. Then with plenty of water, it should be rubbed and squeezed.
If the flow of blood continues after the leeches fall off and it is seeping out, then soak a linen cloth in cold water and place it on top until the seepage stops.
If the blood flows excessively, then powder and sprinkle ground zegi, or galls, and similar astringents over it until the bleeding stops.
Or let halves of peeled beans be placed on the site: and leave them until the beans adhere in place, for the blood is congealed.
And it is necessary if the repetition of bloodletting is required, when possible, to change the places.
If bloodletting is prohibited due to suspense, then the area should be cleaned with fresh blood. Or pierce with a needle in the places until some blood comes out. Then they should be placed, for they adhere immediately when they sense some blood.
When therefore you want them to fall off, sprinkle something over them from ground aloe, or salt, or ash: and they will fall off immediately.
This Chapter is also often necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractured and dislocated bones.
Know, O Sons, that foolish doctors and the common folk boast about this Chapter, and those who have never examined an ancient book or read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method is deprived from the sciences in our land. And I never entirely complied with that good thing, and did not attain from it what I attained except through the length of reading ancient books, and studying their understanding, until I extracted that knowledge from them.
Then I adhered to experience and study throughout my life.
And now I have described to you from that in this Chapter all that my knowledge contains: and beyond what my experience has passed, after I have shortened it for you, and freed it from the burden of lengthiness, and shortened it to the end of abbreviation, and shown it to the end of deviation: and I formed many shapes in it from the forms of instruments which are used in it, since it is by the addition of deviation, as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
We must say in the prologue of this Chapter the summaries of the discourse and the chapters to whose understanding the artisan is first compelled to stand on their truth: that is, so that you and those who are quick may know or learn this noble art and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or fall happens to someone, then you must first hurry to bleed him and to relieve him, or both at the same time: unless something prohibits it, such as weakness of strength; or he to whom one of these things happened is a child or an aged infirm person; or it is a time of extreme heat or extreme cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, and flesh of birds, and of kids: and prohibit wine, and thick meats, and being filled by foods and all food which fills the veins with blood. Until when you are safe from a warm abscess, and do not fear the discharge of matter to the limb: then may the sick person return to his initial regimen with which his habituation has become accustomed.
When therefore the broken bone begins to heal, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods, rich in nutrition, thick, heavy, which have viscosity: such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and roots, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things. With this regimen, the fracture is healed faster and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in their early old age, and the infirm, it is not possible for them to be healed and consolidated according to their natural way, because of the dryness of their limbs: and they are consolidated and continued which are at the end of softening, like the bones of small children.
However, nature attaches something similar to glue over the broken bone from all sides, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds itself, until they are glued together and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its functions: and for this reason it is necessary to provide the sick person's nourishment with foods that have heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And you shall know that the species of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of limbs: for the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the skull bone: and the fracture of the chest bone is different from the fracture of the back bone: and similarly all other limbs exist different from each other: and I come with the recollection of every species of fracture exposed in its Chapter distinct from another. And the species of bone fractures also differ among themselves.
For its fracture is curved, besides which shards may occur in it. And sometimes its fracture is along the length of the bone: and its fractures are splintered, that is, shards, and projections proceeding, as they are in a reed, when it is cut with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture, a small fissure.
And each species has its own method or instrument for its restoration, according to which its recollection will come, distinguished in their respective places.
And by which the fracture of the bone is known, is its twisting and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor sound at your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the patient find great pain: then there is no fracture there, but it is possible that there is a twisting or a small fissure: therefore it is not necessary to move it with hand and compression at all, but place upon it from the medicines of which its recollection comes, after the moment which befits the place: then bind with a fine bandage.
And you shall know that when the place is bent and split into two, apart from the pus that may occur in it, however, all parts are removed from their companion: then it is necessary for you to begin at your appointed time for its correction, equalizing it before a warm abscess befalls it.
And if a warm abscess occurs in it, then leave it for several days until the abscess rests. Then equalize it according to whichever method can be done based on its mildness and ingenuity.
And you shall know that the restoration and equalization of it is easier than that of bone in which pus occurs.
Then bind it according to how the memory of it will come to you.
If pus is in the bone, then the extension of this fractured member from its two parts is necessary, if it is a hand or foot: or with your hand if the member is small: or with two ropes, or with one rope and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the member on an equal ground according to its natural form, until when the parts of the fractured member are extended: then strive to restore those additions with all ingenuity and in whatever way you can to their places: and be careful according to your strength, lest excessive weakness result from your operation, neither pain nor injury: and strive according to your strength to apply one of the two bones with its companion according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time for you to test and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the extent of your skill.
And beware strong extension and forceful compression, as many foolish people do. For they often cause a warm abscess or contraction in the limb through their operation: as we have already seen many times from their procedures.
Then apply after equalization, and proper treatment, and constriction of that limb, and rest, and tranquility: and advise the patient about it. And make him beware not to move it during his waking hours, and in his sleep regarding his turning, and stirring, and in his evacuation, and all his movements with his utmost strength. And let him be cautious that the position of the limb does not cause pain. Which means that when he feels pain or injury in the position of the limb: then it is necessary to change it to another position where he does not feel pain.
And be careful with it, so that its position is equal and straight: so that no distortion occurs in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the constriction of the fractured limb is as I narrate.
Know that fractured limbs differ in their smallness, and their magnitude, and their form.
Therefore, those among them that are small, such as the arm, and finger, and toe, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of a soft, fine cloth. Which of them are thick, like the thigh and leg, must have a wide, hard wrapping.
For the wide bandage connects the large limb and tightens it equally on all sides so that no spot enters it.
Then begin after your evacuation to apply a suitable liniment to that, on soft tow over the site of the fracture. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the site of the fracture with three or four layers, according to what the limb deserves: and hold your hand firmly with the bandage.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the site of the fracture, and hold less tightly than your previous tightening of the fractured site. Then move away with the wrapping from the site of the fracture gradually, and loosen the tightness little by little until you reach a large part from the healthy area.
Then take another strip and wrap it again over the fractured area with layers. Then proceed with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the tightness of the wrapping and its loosening according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings with soft tow or cloth, with which the curvature of the fracture is evened if there is any curvature: and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Then apply the splints evenly over these wraps immediately, if there is no swelling or warm abscess in the place.
If there is swelling or an abscess, then place on it something that will sit on the abscess and reduce the swelling; and leave it for several days. Then tighten the splints over it.
And the splints should be made from the halves of a broad, shaved, and wisely shaped cane; or they may be made from wood used for sieves, which is made from pine, or geraid, that is, palm branches, or calingi: and the width of each splint, according to this form, should have the same shape.
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed over the traction be thicker and slightly wider than the other splints: and the length of the splint should be according to what is suitable for the member in size and smallness.
Then wrap another strip over the splints according to the same tightness as the first one. Then bind it with strings appropriately, according to what we have said about tightness: and it is so that your binding over the site of the fracture is tighter: and the further it extends from the fracture, the looser the binding should be.
And it is necessary that the strings be of medium thickness and fineness, and they should be made of soft linen.
For if the strings are thick, as I have seen in the work of foolish ones who use twisted linen cords, it is a great mistake: for the tightness with them falls outside the proper quality.
And fine threads again do not suit: because you do not achieve with them what you want through tension.
And it should not be less than a finger's width between the stake and the stake.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the stakes after they are tightened at healthy sites: then place under them soft tow or carded wool until nothing is injured from that.
When there is a fracture with a wound, and the skin is ruptured, I will come to it with a reminder of that in the second chapter by its particularity.
And you should know that not every fractured limb should be bound with stakes on the first day. This is because when the limb is large, you should not place stakes on it until after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the risk of hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients restored fractures and dislocations.
Method of a brief common plaster restoring fractures, suitable for most complexions, especially women and children: because it is such that heat does not overcome it nor cold.
And it is done by taking the millstone dust, which is the flour residue hanging on the walls of the mill near the motion of the millstone: and prepare it as it is, except that you sieve it with egg white: and place the mixture not too thick nor too thin. Then use it.
Method of another plaster for restoring fractures, dislocations, and torsions. Take almess, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugwort: and the juice of all ʒ X myrrh, and aloes both ʒ V and alcohol ʒ XX are to be ground, sieved, and mixed with alcohol: or with egg white, if the complexion of the patient is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Method of another plaster which also benefits fractures and torsions. Take mugwort, and fenugreek, and white hollyhock, of each ʒ X, myrrh, and aloes both ʒ V, acacia ʒ VI, Armenian bole ʒ XX. All are to be ground finely, sieved, mixed with water or egg white: and administered.
Method of a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places: and to alleviate pain occurring in them, and bone裂纹和骨折。 Take suet wool and soak it in vinegar and boiled oil: and place it on the area.
And in this plaster there is no restorative power, but it is good for soothing a hot abscess: and it properly relieves pain.
The method of the plaster which restores the broken bone. Take the leaves of the fig of the deaf, and the leaves of the wild poppy: and grind all, and make a plaster from both.
The method of the shortened plaster, applied for the restoration of the fractured bone: and when you want the resolution of the remaining abscess. Take the root of althea, and chamomile, and flowers of violet, and flour of the herb, of all one part each: grind all, and prepare with wine, if the limb is not heated.
If it is heated, then prepare it with water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
The method of another stronger plaster for resolution, than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs at the restoration of the limb. Take the root of althea, and seeds of flax, and pomegranate flowers, and sweet clover, and marshmallow, and flowers of violet, and chamomile, of all one part each: grind all, and prepare with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. Use the whole according to the heat of the member, and the cooling of its temperature.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything should be placed on the fractured limb during its restoration, except a plaster made of wax and oil: nothing else: and he described it to be a balance between thickness and fineness.
But Galen thought that over the fractured limb during its restoration should be placed things which have a drying heat, such as myrrh, and aloe and frankincense, and similar to these of those which we have narrated.
Method of the plaster that helps with the wound and pain. Take myrrh, and chickpeas, and human hair that has been cut, or a bird's feather, and coltsfoot, and salt in equal parts: they are ground, sifted, and prepared: and make a plaster from it.
The quantity according to which it is necessary for the bandage to remain, and then be loosened, is such that you should consider whether there has not occurred to the patient pain, nor itching, nor movement of the fractured bone from its place: then do not loosen it for many days. If there occurs to him a severe itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling in the place: then hurry and loosen it within an hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, wet: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides.
If there occurs to him a severe itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling in the place: then hurry and loosen it within an hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, wet: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, on it: and bind it for one night, until you are sure there is no warm abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then bind it with a fine compression and a small plaster: and do not constrict it with your first binding, and use fine bindings until it heals.
If you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all symptoms are now completely removed, and you need a plaster and binding, then do it again as you did at first equally.
If nothing of what we have said has happened to the limb, then do not loosen it, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is left for twenty days, do all of that according to what appears to you from loosening the limb as we said, until the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh approximates over it: then add more in binding than your whole first binding: and also increase the infirm man's food according to his previous recollection of it.
If you see the place of the fracture already drying out and becoming thinner than it should be: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. So fumigate it with warm water every time you loosen it, and do this for all three days, and lighten the binding momentarily.
Through this operation, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
That which foolish restorers do with a fractured limb by some other means when it is not restored at first as it should be, and is restored with a twist, is an error from their operation, and it is a great fear: and if it were right, the ancients would have said it in their books and acted upon it.
And I did not see any trace of them in it at all, and it is better that it not be done.
Of the treatment of an accident to the head.
The types of fractures are diverse, and its shapes are many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture caused by a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the entire bone until it reaches the saphac, which is beneath the bone, just as an axe affects wood: and because of this, this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or the sword cuts through part of the bone, and cuts its surface, but the cut does not penetrate to the end of it: and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, which means Absolute Elevation.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a grinding or wearing down: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which approaches the membrane beneath the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and it is a wound of these two fractures, either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone like a hair's thickness: and it is a small crack, and because of this, this type of fracture is called Capillary.
And among them is a fracture caused by a fall or a blow with a stone, and similar things, which cause the bone plate to extend into the interior, and a concavity is formed: just as happens to bellows of brass when they are struck and part of them extends inward.
And that happens very much in the heads of humid bones, as are the heads of children.
And separate efforts are made for all these types of fractures, and I will come with a recollection of the whole of that in its proper place.
And we know all these types of fractures through examination of them, and inquiry into them with probes, and removal of the corrupted flesh which is on them.
The capillary type is recognized by this, that it is detected on the bone, cleaned, and anointed on it with encaustic: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the symptoms of the patient: then if you see from the symptoms that it clearly indicates fear, such as choleric vomiting, and extension, and loss of intellect, and cessation of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar ones among the symptoms in most dispositions, undoubtedly it is not cured: and if you see symptoms that do not terrify you, and hope for recovery: then begin its treatment.
Which means, if a wounded person comes to you when first wounded: and it is during the winter months, then it is necessary that you focus on the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all dispositions.
And if it is during the summer months, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone from the membrane becomes corrupted, and those symptoms we have mentioned occur.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and it is with fragments, and traction: then it is necessary that you cut away the shattered and worn part, as I tell you.
And it is so that you should scrape the head of the wounded area: and expose over the bone in whatever way is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light over the injury.
If during the exposure of the bone there happens to be a flow of blood or a warm abscess: then apply what is necessary to it. And it is so that you should fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest until the abscess settles, and be secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with an instrument of a sharp edge: and this is its form.
Then apply another cutter after the first, somewhat wider than the previous one: and this is its form.
Then apply another cutter even wider than the second one.
And the second method is to have a number of different cutters with you: some of which are wider than others, and some of which are shorter than others: and they should be sharp at their ends, and they should be made of Indian iron or steel: and use them easily in striking over the bone, so that it does not shake the head and harm it.
If the bone is strong, hard: then you must pierce around it before you apply the cutting instruments with drills which are called non-deepening drills: and they are not so named unless because they do not penetrate the limit of the fleshy bone to that which is behind it: therefore, the drill has a round end above that which is under its sharp head, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents submersion, and penetration through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take from these drills a great number, each of which should be suitable to the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill appropriate for every skull: the sharpness of whose ends in length and brevity should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three species of drills, or trephines.
The quality of piercing around the fractured bone is: to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then change the drill to another place, and place it so that there is the width of a radius or about that between every hole.
Then cut with the cutting instruments what is between every hole from the bone: and make it finer than you can at the lighter end, as we have said, until you root out the bone, either by hand or with some other of the instruments you have prepared for this: such as forceps and fine chisels.
And you must be cautious with all care that the drill or cutting instrument does not touch anything from the sinew.
Therefore, when you drill around the tooth and separate it from the gum, if it is connected to it, and you extract it, you must file it and even out the end that remains in the bone with another similar chisel-like instrument. However, it must be thinner and finer than the other chisels.
If any small pieces of bone remain and cause trouble, then remove them easily with the instruments you have. Then treat the wound after that with licinis and the ointments we have mentioned.
And another method of the operation is easy, entirely free from fear: which Galen mentioned and praised highly: and this is his teaching. It is necessary for you to begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is more severe and evident: until you expose that part and place under it the end of this lens-shaped chisel, which has this form.
And the lens-shaped part, being blunt, does not cut anything: and the sharp part of it running along the two sides in length, as you see: so that the lens-shaped part rests against the gum, with its sharp face facing the bone.
Then strike on the chisel from one side with a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone easily, as it turns: and you shall be secure about the flap, that no accident will happen to it which you fear entirely.
And if the medic were not stupider than others and more ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the flap in some places, he would cut it off with the end of the lens-shaped chisel and free it easily. For it is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the panniculus, and that which is severed from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and fine splinters remain in it: then you must scrape away that roughness, and root out those splinters with subtle rasps of various sizes which you have prepared, so that you may be able to use the most suitable and appropriate ones among them according to what the operation leads you to do, and the bone itself, and its shape. And you must apply yourself with reason, whereby you first scrape the bone more widely with those rasps. Then use the finer one after it.
And you must apply yourself with reason, whereby you first scrape the bone more widely with those rasps. Then use the finer one after it. And do not cease to do this continuously until you reach the point of using the finest and most subtle of them all.
In each of the remaining small capillary fissures and fine fractures, you must apply care according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a matter that is not hidden from anyone who has any interest in this art, and who has stood over what we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the panniculus detected is retained when you remove the bone: then you must take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and make it as light as you can on top of it, so that it is not too heavy.
Then apply over the broad ligament: and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds the wound only: and leave it with this care for one day or two days, until you are secure from warm abscess. Then loosen it, and apply after it medications with which there is excessive dryness: such as lily roots, and flour of the herb, and incense powder, and aristolochia, and similar to these: and it is that you make from these medications a powder, which is dusted over the wound like they are dry.
And universally apply to it all medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to irritate. And be careful according to your ability, that the wound in your care is clear: nor should there be any ointment dirt or filth in it.
And be careful according to your ability, that the wound in your care is clear: nor should there be any ointment dirt or filth in it. And do not allow pus to accumulate in it at all: for when pus accumulates on the brain membrane, it corrupts and putrefies it, and great harm befalls the patient afterwards: and it often happens in scalp infections, when the bone is cleansed of it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore, consider then, if the patient experiences the symptoms we have mentioned, you shall know that it is certainly dangerous.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine placed upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness: then you must take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and wet the cloth with them. Then place him on a shiffin: afterwards care for him with the types of treatment which are appropriate, until he is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when fractured.
You should know that only the two upper parts of the nose are fractured simultaneously or one of them: because there are two bones; since its lower part is cartilaginous, it does not fracture, and what happens to it is only wear, distortion, and flattening.
If one of the two parts is fractured, then you must insert a small finger into the nostril and align that fracture with your index finger and thumb from outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape: and this should be done skillfully, with ease. And beware that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then it should be aligned using the end of a rod, which should have a small thickness.
If the fracture is in both parts, then do the same: and hurry with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day when the inflammation has subsided.
Then insert a strip of linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part: or insert two strips if the fracture is in both parts: and let the strip have a thickness that fills the nostril.
And some of the ancients said that quicksilver should be poured into butter, and changed every day. That indeed does not seem right to me; rather, it should be poured into the curd of sheep's milk, mixed with mill dust. Then apply the quicksilver until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of quicksilver, after being wrapped with a soft cloth; and it happens that it holds the nasal fracture very firmly, and does not prevent the patient from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that: and if you wish, use quicksilver.
And if the nose is affected again while you are treating a warm abscess, apply plasters to the nose with cerote, or with cuttlefish bone infused in rose oil, or a little dyaciton.
But if a warm abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to apply plasters made with sesame flour and incense powder mixed with sheep's curd. Then place a soft piece of tow on it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
But if the bones of the nose are fractured slightly or shattered, then it is necessary to incise over them and extract them with suitable instruments. Then sew the incision and treat it with what promotes fleshing and strengthening, using appropriate plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nose, then you must treat it with rushes: and administer a lead pipe until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not with a wound: then examine if the fracture is only on the outer side and is not broken into two, and is bent inward: then its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient: similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand: and push with it the prominence of the fracture from inside to the outside easily with your other hand from the inner part of the bone properly, and straighten it with it.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then you must apply extension from both sides, according to straightness: until it is possible to align it.
If, however, the teeth have already been shaken or broken: then bind those that you hope will remain with gold thread, or silver thread, or silk. Then place wax over the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and put a well-made large splint on top of the cloth: or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the jaw.
Then bandage it according to how its binding is prepared and its application suits, until it is not destroyed: and instruct the patient to maintain tranquility and rest: and give him a diet of mild sorptions.
If however you think that something has already changed in its appearance in some way: then hurry and treat it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and make a poultice of mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the scabs: and place soft wool on the poultice.
Therefore, while that poultice remains on it and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it alone and do not remove it until it heals: or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound in three weeks.
If during this time a hot abscess occurs, then apply what we have said many times for its reduction, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, take note. For if a fragment is already separated from the bone or from the soft tissue: then be precise in this, to remove those fragments from the soft tissue using whatever instruments are suitable for their removal.
If however the wound opening is narrow, then enlarge it with a lancet according to the amount necessary for you.
Then when you have removed those soft tissues and nothing remains of them, sew the wound opening if it is large: and if not, apply a poultice suitable for it, and promote flesh growth until it heals.
Regarding the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
The collarbone is mostly fractured from the front part near the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two parts, besides which contusions may occur: and it is easier to restore it.
Or it is such that contusions occur in the bone: and it is more difficult to restore.
Or it is such that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation for it when the fracture is without a wound is to have two assistants present, so that one of them holds an aid following the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers until its form becomes as it should be: and there should not be any prominence or concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place a sphere of cloth or wool under the patient's neck. The size should be according to your needs: and extend and lift the collarbone, and compress the sphere with your hand until you equalize the fracture as it should be.
If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outward because it has gone deep, then it is necessary to lay the patient on their back with their neck down. Place a medium-sized pillow under his shoulder and have an assistant press his shoulder downward until the end of the collarbone that is deep rises upward. Then correct and equalize the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel a splinter from the collarbone and it moves, then you must cut over it and remove the splinter with ease.
If it is retained in the bone, then you must apply yourself to cutting it with one of the files which you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the collarbone an instrument that preserves the skin; and it is an instrument made of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that does not have a concavity.
And its width should be according to what you need from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length should also be according to what is possible for you in performing the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see: one end should be broad, and the other narrow.
If the disruption of the wound which you made when you extracted the bone splinter is wide and threatens to become inflamed with warm swelling: then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear warm swelling: then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the amount of the wound's tear.
If a warm swelling does occur, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on the swelling.
A constriction of the bone when it is without a wound and without a cut is treated by placing on the bone a poultice made from mill flour with egg white. And place a soft cloth over it. Then place the ball under his scrotum, if you need that. Then take the himematem, that is, the roll which the Saracens use, which should be very long: and its width should be one palm, or about that. Then place a doubled poultice over the cloth and plaster. Then take a strip from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the strip in place of the himematem which falls over the fracture and area. Then place the himematem over the fracture as wrapped, and wrap it around his neck, and under his healthy scrotum, and under his injured scrotum: and bring it back over all parts, and how you see that the binding holds the fracture with proper tightness: and this is what is not hidden from you, and all of it, so that the strip is not removed from over the broken end. And for this reason, it is necessary to look after the patient every day. Whenever the bandage softens and you see that the strip can now be removed: then adjust it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's scrotum on his back, and put a small pillow under his scrotum at his side during the night, so that his support is lifted from beside him. The fracture of the furcula is raised by elevating the shoulder.
Either bind his arm to his neck: and do not loosen the bandage, if no accident occurs from itching or an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see that it is needed. And add to the binding, and leave it until it is restored and healed.
And the fracture of the furcula is bound and strengthened to the utmost within twenty-eight days: and sometimes it happens in some men in a shorter time.
Regarding the restoration of the fracture of the spatula.
The spatula is rarely fractured, and if fractured, it is at its wide part: and only the edges of it are broken.
When therefore that part is fractured, and it is broken in the middle of it, and it is not recognizable except by touch: then according to the shape of the fracture attempt its preparation. And restore it to its natural shape by any means of skill that is possible for you.
Then apply mill powder with egg white to the area, and a soft tow, and place a poultice made of doubled cloth over it. Then place a wide strip from a thin board over it according to the size of the entire spatula, or slightly larger than it.
If however there is a concavity in some places of the spatula, then equalize that concavity with soft tow, until the needle descends according to equality.
Then bind over it with linen bandages, with appropriate constriction, and the firmness of the needle, until it is not removed from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage is softened, tighten it, and equalize the needle if it is moved from its place: and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When this number is complete, then loosen the bandage: and you will be in safety. For it is made of bones whose joining is not feared, nor their separation.
If however a fragment protrudes from the bone and pricks under the skin: then cut over it, and remove it. And do what we have said in the case of a clavicle fracture from the settling of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
Regarding the restoration of a fractured rib.
The chest is sometimes fractured in its middle, and this rarely occurs. Its extremities are often fractured and crushed from its accidents.
When its middle is broken, which inclines towards the lower parts, and there occurs intense pain to it, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps he vomits blood, and a concavity is evident in the broken bone, and not hidden from sense, Its restoration is for the sick person to lie supine on his back: and place between its spatulas his pillow.
Then press his shoulders, and bring the ribs together from both sides or sides with your hands: and adjust in its alignment, according to every way that it is possible and appropriate, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then place upon it a plaster and tow, and place above a slender stick from willow board, or chalingi, and those similar in lightness, after you wrap it in cloth. Then adjust in binding it over the fractured bone, so that it does not move: and go with the bandage around to the back several times, and secure it with a proper tightness.
Then examine the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it: and if the necessity of it provokes its removal due to itching, or corrosion which occurs in the place, or pain, or swelling: then hurry and remove it, and take off the plaster, and correct what happens according to the method of its treatment.
Then apply the plaster again if you see that it is necessary: and secure it with a bandage until it heals.
On the restoration of ribs when they are fractured.
Know that the fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts following the back, and from their extremities on the front side. And wear and tear does not affect them, unless because they are made of cartilage. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense during the examination with fingers.
And its restoration is done by aligning the fracture with fingers as much as possible until the shape is even, as it should be. Then apply plasters and bind the fractured ends with a splint, if it requires one.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the interior: then the patient suffers intense pain and puncturing, like the puncturing that occurs in one who has pleurisy: therefore, because the bone pierces the membrane, and also the patient suffers difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting much blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have devised many ingenious methods for this: among them are those who say that you should give the patient foods that generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the stomach becomes inflamed, expands, and pushes the fracture towards the exterior.
We, however, abstain from causing a quick formation of a hot abscess unless it occurs. If it has already occurred, then address it accordingly.
And some of them have said: place something suction-like over the area, then suck it with strength, and it is similar in reasoning. However, it is feared that it attracts windy excesses to the place because of the condition of weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been submerged in warm oil: and poultices should be placed between the ribs, until it is filled: so that the bandage is even when you wrap it according to the roundness.
Then the sick person should be treated for pleurisy with food and medicine.
If a strong thing that the sick person cannot tolerate constrains them, and the cloth pierces the mouth with a damaging puncture, and we are concerned for the sick person: then it is necessary to cut above the place and expose from the broken rib. Then we place upon it the instrument that sews the lips, of which the description has preceded: and we cut the lip with ease, and incise it.
Then we bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture: and we treat it with plasters until it heals.
If a hot abscess should occur in the meantime, then hurry and pour poultices into rose oil, and place them on the area: and treat the sick person with what is appropriate for the abscess.
In the meantime, he should also lie on his side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Regarding the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
The vertebrae of the neck are sometimes fractured, and rarely do they suffer from wear; similarly for the vertebrae of the back. When therefore this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not healed: then make your judgment. For if you see that his hands are already softened, numb, and dead: and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them: and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then you know that he will not be healed, and is in a condition that is mortal in most cases.
But if he moves them and feels the contraction with fingers and the pricking: then you know that the bone's function is intact, and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
If a similar condition befalls the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then look at his feet: if you see that they are softened, and what happened to the hands happens to them as well: then when he lies on his back, flatulence and excretion escape involuntarily: and when he is placed on his stomach, excretion escapes involuntarily: and when he is laid on his back and wishes to urinate but cannot: then you know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
If however none of that happens to him, the case is lighter.
And the treatment for what happens from that is to endeavor the cooling of the inflammation: so that you place on the worn vertebra rose oil alone: or with eggs of a roasted hen.
Place it on the same several times a day until the warm abscess is calmed; then place one of the strengthening, drying, and binding plasters on the area and bind it with a bandage: and instruct the patient to use rest and tranquility. Let him not sleep on the side where he feels pain until it is healed.
If it happens near the worn bone or something that is already separated from it, then it is necessary to cut over the skin and remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture if it is large. Then treat it with some flesh-forming ointment until it is healed.
If the last bone of the tail is fractured and it is the end of the tail: then you must insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible, and perform the second alignment.
Then place it on the area and remove it: and treat the wound with the preceding treatment until it is healed.
Regarding the restoration of the anchor.
Chapter Eleven.
The bones of the ankles rarely fracture. If they are fractured, then the fracture is not complete but a comminution at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise and displaced inward: and the patient experiences pain and swelling in the area, and the leg that is fractured becomes numb.
And its restoration is that you extend your hand over it until you stand over the fracture how its figure is.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then equalize that fracture according to what is prepared for you by the equation until it resembles its natural figure.
If the fracture is along the length, or already inclines towards the interior: then make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When therefore you have equalized it, place a poultice on it: then place a splint of wood or of hide on it: and bind with a bandage on which you do not fear the displacement of the fracture nor the removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of it with what fills it until it is taken by the bandage according to equality: and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
If a warm abscess happens to him: then cease from extending it and its restoration until the warm abscess is seated: and place on it what seats it, as precedes, then return to its restoration: and bind it as necessary.
If, however, there is pus in the bone or it is crushed, extract it.
Regarding the restoration of the Adiutorius fracture.
The Adiutorium is that which is between the elbow and the head of the Spatula.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: to take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form that follows.
And let two bands be tied at the two ends.
Then it should be suspended from an elevated place: and the patient should sit on a seat. Then he should throw his broken arm over the wood until it reaches the tip of it, where the bend of the wood is attached in the middle.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that it may stretch downwards. Then the physician equalizes the fracture with his hands simultaneously, until the fracture returns to its proper position.
And the other method is to have the patient lie on their back: and suspend his arm from his neck with a band. Then command two assistants, so that one of them holds what is above the fracture with his hands: and the other holds below it, and each one extends to their respective part.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with one band, and above it with another band: and each of the two assistants should extend them to their respective parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the shoulder joint, then it is necessary to place one band below the tip and another below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a bandage be placed on that place, and on the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without effort, until when the fracture is equalized as it should be, and is applied with good alignment: then bind it, if a warm abscess does not occur.
If a warm abscess occurs, then release the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess quiets: then bind it: and the method of binding it is to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster. Then let the arm be bound with support, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind the arm with support, so that the arm remains in its proper place: if nothing prohibits you from it, nor anything alters from the fractured bone on you.
If you fear that something from the fractured bone may alter on you, then use splints: and it is to place a splint over the fracture itself which should be wider and stronger than the other splints. And place the width of a finger between each splint: and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture with an additional three fingers on every side.
Then bind over the splints with the bandage as I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your binding on the place of the fracture should be tighter. And the more the fracture is lengthened, the binding should be looser.
If you see the placement of the splints and the binding as we have said, at the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do so: and if you fear a warm abscess, then remove the binding and the splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then observe the binding for all three days, so that itching or swelling does not occur, or food is prevented from reaching the limb due to the excess tightness of the binding. Therefore, correct all of that according to what we have said.
If however you are confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not loosen the binding unless after three days, or more.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his hand on his stomach: and place under the support a cushion filled with wool evenly.
And observe at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the fractured limb is not loosened or the binding softened. Therefore, correct all of that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have mentioned, so that the food is light at first, until the wound begins to heal: then it is necessary to make his food more substantial.
For it is customary for the support and leg to be bound for forty days: therefore, it must be loosened, and a bath administered, and treated with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is ugly, ground down: then do not remove the bandage and splint until fifty days, and until two months.
On the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the humerus.
Of which one is small: and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed below the small one on the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger bone is fractured, or the smaller one: and perhaps both are fractured at the same time.
When therefore the smaller bone is fractured above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower bone is fractured, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse it is when the two bones are fractured at the same time.
If therefore the bone that is fractured is the small upper one, then it is fitting for the doctor in its restoration to apply a slight extension with ease, until it is straightened.
If however the large bone is the one that is fractured: then it is necessary to apply a much stronger binding.
And it is necessary to place the hand in a position during its restoration, and to extend it on a pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lower joint be common to all the fingers or the rest: and the patient sitting squarely on himself, and let the pillow be before him, or directly in front of him, in height, so that the patient does not incur labor.
Then let the minister extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And another minister shall extend similarly from above.
Then the doctor equalizes the bone until he restores it to the best shape possible.
If there are fragments in the fractured bone, then endeavor to reduce every fragment to its place by your skill.
If there appears a separate piece of bone protruding and piercing the skin, and you have no hope for its restoration: then incise over it, and remove it according to the method we have described in the preceding.
If the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter on this, so take the treatment from there.
If a warm abscess should occur at the beginning of your restoration, then line cloth with a plaster made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be neither too thick nor too thin: and bind the cloth tightly over it with a fine bandage until the abscess is calmed; then remove the plaster, and apply an ointment prepared from mill powder with egg white. Then place the splints, and let the splint that is placed over the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger.
And you shall know that the number of stars in the arm is six in most dispositions: whether the fracture be in one socket or in two sockets at once.
Then place your binding over the site of the fracture stronger and more forceful, and somewhat more with the binding toward the upper part of the fracture, or towards the lower part of it: or place a milder binding for a while according to how the preceding recollection at the beginning of the chapter dictates.
And let the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture be soft cloths, moist; and not hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be of flax, properly medium between fine and thick, as we have mentioned.
And consider the limb and the binding after days. And if something happens which requires correction, such as itching which occurs in the limb: then it is necessary to foment the limb with warmed water until that itching ceases, and leave the limb unconstricted until it rests, then bind it again.
And if the binding has already been softened, and the bone has already been removed, and things like that: then correct all of it with your skill, and examine again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the binding, then it is necessary to soften it for a while and leave it for several days until nourishment flows to it, then bind it again.
But if none of the things we have mentioned happen to the patient, then it is not necessary to remove the binding unless after twenty days, or around this time.
Then let the patient's hand be suspended at his neck. And let his neck be steady, and protected by its strength from disturbing motions. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of that depends on the dispositions of complexions and the dispositions of strength.
Regarding the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the knuckles of the hand and the joints of the fingers. And it does not happen to him, except through frequent attrition.
When therefore the fracture or attrition occurs to the palm, then it is necessary that the patient sit upright, and before him be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand extended on it.
Then let the assistant extend the fractured bones, and let the doctor align them until they are joined well together.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, if a warm abscess does not occur. Then place a splint on top according to the length of the area: and you have now wrapped it in a linen cloth.
If however the fracture is near the lower part around the heel, then make something like a sphere from cloth: and instruct the patient to bind it with his fractured foot: then wrap it with a long cloth.
And let the splint be made of leather, which should have softness, so that the leather may be applied with the body of the foot: and bind it according to what is necessary.
If however the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary to place a splint on top, and another splint below on the foot: and let the hand be open straight. Then place the binding, as the hand is rotated: and extend between the toes with a band.
If a fracture occurs to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, align it according to what is necessary. Then bind it with the sole: and if you wish, place a straight, small splint on it: so that the fracture is raised, and not moved.
If however the fracture is to the other fingers, such as the middle finger, and index, or little finger, and ring finger, then align it, and bind it with the healthy finger that follows it: or bind all according to continuity: and this is better. Or place a small splint on it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposal of a warm abscess: and oppose with what is necessary when something from it occurs, according to how its narrative is repeated.
On the restoration of the fractured hip.
The hip bone is often fractured, and it is noticeable because it turns inward and backward.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a band is applied above the fracture, and another band below the fracture. And the patient should lie on his face.
Then the surgeon should stretch all the band to its part according to equality: and this is done when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
If the fracture is near the root of the hip bone, then the band should be placed at the part of the pectineus muscle, so that the extension falls upward: and another band below the fracture.
And thus similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then the band should be placed near the knee, so that the extension is downward.
Then the doctor equalizes the bone with both of his hands until he restores it to its natural shape: and the bone is joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and binding, if swelling does not occur in the limb. If swelling does occur: then leave it for several days until the warm swelling subsides, then return to your treatment.
The binding should be done in this way. You must wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, and fold it twice or three times, leaving some excess. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the root of the thigh.
And insert a long thread between the hip and leg near the knee to the lower part, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of both sides. Then roll over the leg and hip what is excess from the bandage.
Then place on the hip at the site of the fracture the splints, and put one of them over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that it equalizes the binding.
Then begin with a strong binding from the middle above the site of the fracture with three or four windings. And the further you extend with the bandage from the site of the fracture, let your binding be looser and softer, and gentler.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the hip and leg. Bind therefore with both three splints which are on top. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread to the lower part until you reach the ankle. And with both pull also the ends of the splints from the other side so that the binding does not move from its place.
Then apply the binding over it while it remains, in such a way that no corrosion, abscess, swelling, or similar things occur to the member. If something happens from it. Hurry therefore with its solution, and correct everything that happens from it, according to what we have said many times.
If however there is a fragment in the bone that pierces from the bone: then it is necessary to equally remove it, if it is possible for you. And if not, find it and remove it: and treat it according to the preceding instructions until it is healed.
And sometimes this hip fracture is bound, besides adding splints to it, as we have said in the case of support and arm.
However, in the restoration of it thus fractured, the patient does not suffer from limping. And if it is restored alone, besides applying the other leg to it, then it is necessary that the one to whom it belongs limps always.
And know that the hip is bound for fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is subtracted: all of this depends on the diversity of complexions and other conditions.
Regarding the restoration of the fractured kneecap.
Know that fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but wear often happens to it.
If however a fracture does occur to it, then it is only either a crack or a hole in its parts. And it is with wound, and without wound. And it stands over the whole with feeling.
And the restoration of it is such that the parts which are torn are equalized with fingers until they aggregate and are joined as much as possible by equality and ease, and construction.
Then place a poultice of sesame flour, or from the mill, with egg white: and place a round splint on it if you need that: and bind it tightly with an appropriate bandage.
Then consider all the treatments we have mentioned in other fractures: such as a warm abscess, and similar ones: so that you oppose every occurrence with what is appropriate to it until it heals.
Regarding the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the legs are two bones, of which one is thick and is named the name of the leg: and the other is slender, and is named Fibula.
And it happens to both species of fractures as it happens to the two bones of the arm: and because of this, its restoration is like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is the same.
If both bones are fractured simultaneously, the leg turns in all directions.
And if the slender bone is fractured, the leg turns to the front part.
And if the thicker bone is fractured, you find it below: and it is one of those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and equalization, and ligation of the stars according to that equally.
However, it is necessary, if the fracture of the leg is a nasty fracture, having many septic signs: that there be less extension, and milder alleviation by you.
And in the leg indeed there is something from the operation for the arm: and it is that when you equalize the stars, and complete all your operation, then take two bands from pine wood, which is used in the fabrication of solariums which is placed under boards: and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from them those which have small thickness, and let them not be from the thin ones: and their length should be according to the length of the leg from the knee downward.
Then wrap over each one two layers of cloth according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and they should be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind the two bands at three places from both ends and the middle. For when constriction is prohibited, the leg deviates to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now a cradle of wood according to the length of the leg should be administered, and the leg should be placed in it, so that it keeps it from movement.
And it is very necessary that you do this in a fracture when it is accompanied by a proper wound.
Then consider the leg every day, and strive with utmost diligence to remove abscesses, or swellings, or other such things. If any of those things happen, then apply to it what is necessary until it is healed, if God wills.
On the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
A complete fracture does not occur in the heel.
Bones of the foot sometimes suffer a fracture, and toes rarely suffer a fracture as well; and in most cases, they only sustain a bruise.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see those bones protruding above their normal position, then let the patient place his foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot on what has protruded from those bones; then press or tread on it until it is aligned and returns to its proper place.
Then apply a poultice and place some tow over it; and put a small board under the arch of the foot, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it to the foot with a suitable bandage after you wrap it with cloths and other necessary materials.
When three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones aligned whether they are fractured or dislocated. The operation therefore is on them, as you see.
If some fingers are fractured, then restore them, and equalize them as I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place the splint on your finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than that; then place under the foot the board I have told you about, and secure it with a proper binding.
If however what is fractured of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place on every finger a splint of its size, wrapped in a linen cloth: and secure the board at the lower part of the foot: and let it stand on one end of the board outside the bottom of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is fitting for you, so that you do not forget, to consider all that I have told you about other fractures from the accidents we mentioned: and address every accident with what is necessary.
Regarding the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the pubic bone, and a man's virga.
When the vulva of a woman is fractured, then make her sit squarely. Then tilt her to the side of her back slightly, and let her be held backwards.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with cotton until it is full: and let it be in the vulva like a sphere.
Then shake the woman, and raise her back gradually. For that exits the cotum until it becomes like a sphere in front of the Vulva's gate, and the broken mouth returns.
Then place the poultice on her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cotum with ease, then let her urinate: and reintroduce it in the manner you first inserted it; then return to her original poultice.
Let her do this for seven days, or so, for the mouth itself is restored.
And if you wish, take the bladder of a sheep, and attach a reed tube to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her Vulva. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder inflates inside the Vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then it should be filled with cotum and remain in place for several days, as we have mentioned, until it heals.
And when the mouth of the Pectinis of a man or woman is fractured, then administer for its restoration and equalization what we have mentioned for the Anchae bone.
And the rectum is not concealed in these nearby fractures that slightly affect it, especially in one who has some practice or use.
And understand my book greatly, for the fracture itself indicates to you the right way in its restoration and tightening in many dispositions: therefore understand.
When the man's penis is broken, take the neck of a goose and insert the rod into it. Then place a wrapping of cloth on it and wrap it up, and leave it for three days, or around that time, and it will be healed if God wills.
On the restoration of fractured bones when there is an injury.
It is suitable for one to whom a fracture with an injury has occurred, especially if it is severe, such as the hip bone or the scapula or similar, that you should hurry and bleed him immediately: if the conditions for bleeding are evident to you, as we have mentioned before.
If the wound is bleeding, then you must hurry to treat it by dusting ground zegi over it: if you have nothing else available.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it if an abscess has not occurred.
If an abscess does occur that is hot, then postpone the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has settled. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause harmful complications to occur.
If the broken bone protrudes above the skin: then you must try to reduce it and align it with your hands with ease and a slight extension.
If it cannot be extended and aligned with your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And there is an instrument that is made of iron, whose length is seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the size of the wound. And for that reason, it is necessary for the doctor to take three or four of them according to the amount he needs for that part of the treatment in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it does not double under pressure on it during the time of the operation: and let it have a sharp end, which has an edge at its ends. And let the upper part of it be thickened from the middle of it down to the lower part.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation to place its sharp curved end on the protruding bone end: and push it with it once, until the bone returns and is leveled by some alignment.
Therefore, attempt to align the ends of the fracture closely together.
If the ends of it are finely fractured and do not receive good hold by the instrument, then it is necessary to cut that bone end until the instrument can grasp it.
If you cannot reduce them at all with what we have described, then cut it with one of the scalpels we mentioned that is suitable for it: or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
Then scrape what remains on the bone from roughness and small shards.
When therefore the bone is reduced, and the patient finds after its reduction a very severe painful pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do therefore that. For you provide great assistance to the patient when the restoration that you perform on the bone is completed.
Then soak the cloth in black styptic wine. And especially if it is in summer. And you shall not place wax on the wound, nor anything containing oil: so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then apply the splints at the completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound exposed: so that you cut with scissors a hole in the bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be cautious with all care, lest you compress the wound with the fracture. For foolish doctors have often done this: and have caused either death, or corruption, or pus, that is, suppuration, to occur in their patients. And let your binding be gentle, mild, different from the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is severe, or it is a large wound, and you fear some of the bad accidents we have mentioned might occur, and you find pain in the suspended area: then it should not be covered with splints. And make involutions from hard cloths at the site of the splinters, and bind it with them.
When it is after one day or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove the cloth you placed over it with wine. Then apply licinia, and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as the ointment tetrapharmacum, and those similar to it.
And it is appropriate for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every morning and evening until it becomes firm and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus may run to the lower parts with ease.
If many days pass for the wound and it does not become firm, nor does the pus separate from it: then you know that there are small bone growths there. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. How many of those growths have separated, remove them, and extract them. And those among them that are not separated, and prick the limb, and cause pain, attempt to cut them and remove them in any way possible for you.
If gangrene or ecilten, that is, estiomenus, or some other form of corruption and putrefaction befalls the wound: then it is necessary to oppose each of them with what is appropriate to it according to the treatment previously described in its chapter.
And from which my speech must stand, and to whom you should present your mind when a bone is broken, and protrudes over the member, such as the hip bone, and thigh, and similar ones from large members, it is that you do not presume its attraction, for death often results from it, rather leave it until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then therefore treat the wound if it seems right to you: and if not, leave it.
Regarding the treatment of swelling in a fractured limb.
This swelling often occurs in the limb during the healing of a fracture. Especially those near the joints: and the figure of the member is disfigured by it. And it may prevent the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, consider if the swelling is recent: apply to it medications that have astringent properties, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, tragacanth, acacia, and similar ones.
So that you take some of these, or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the swelling with tow, and bind them tightly over it.
And leave the binding, do not loosen it for many days. Then dissolve it, and repeat another, until the knot is removed.
Or bind over the plate of lead appropriately. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the members.
If however the knot has already turned into a stone and is tight, and necessity urgently requires its removal: then find on it from its upper part, and cut off the protruding excess: or scrape it with some scrapers until it is removed: and care for it until it heals.
Regarding the treatment when it is restored, and a subtle member remains afterwards, not according to its natural state.
When the fracture of the bone is restored, and a subtle, weak member remains afterwards: then this does not occur except for many reasons. One of which is the excess of loosening and tying the ligaments not as it should be: or due to the excess of tightness of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prevented from flowing to the member: or due to the excess of application of unnecessary embrocation at an inappropriate time: or due to the lack of blood in the body of the patient and the weakness thereof.
And the treatment for this is to feed the patient and make their body tender until the blood is multiplied in it. And administer a bath. And bring joy and happiness to him.
Then put pitch on the limb: so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. And apply frequent rubbing of it with warm water until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
On the treatment of fractured bones when they heal crookedly.
When it happens that after the restoration and healing of a limb, there is crookedness, prominence of the fractured bone, or knotting, and the form of the member is marred by this, yet the limb is not hindered in its natural function: then you should not heed the advice of one who thinks the limb must be broken again.
And there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed greatly disgraceful, leading to death or great fear, so as to be mortal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent: then it is necessary to apply a warm water rub, in which softening herbs have been decocted, such as the leaves of althea, and its root, and melilot, and things similar to these. And a poultice should be made for it with a softening plaster, such as properly made dyachylon.
Or take the mucilage of the root of althea, and mix it with chicken fat or sesamin oil: and apply a poultice with it.
Either take fat figs, and pound them with pigeon dung, and similar things which are named among medicines diminishing consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is dissolved with frequent, old, and gentle friction, which is done with wet hands. And the movement of the limb should be administered to all parts at all times.
If however the twisting has already preceded, and become strong, and turned into stone, and the necessity has been provoked for its treatment with iron: then it is necessary that the upper part of it be cut, and the continuity of the bone be dissolved, and what is superfluous from the knot or bone be cut with subtle chisels: and ease be administered to that with care and diligence.
Regarding the discussion on dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of some joint from its place. Therefore, it is prohibited from its motion, and the figure of the member is disfigured. And it causes severe pains and injuries to the afflicted.
Therefore, when dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to hurry to its restoration: do not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the area becomes swollen, and possibly the reduction of the dislocation with it.
Therefore, during the swelling, it should not be moved or extended. For it often causes spasms and painful pains.
But when that happens, then you must hurry to bleed the patient. Then it should be left until the swelling subsides.
