Then the limb should be rubbed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And the entire limb should be treated with what will be mentioned in its proper place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocations, even as it preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body to its lower part.
Regarding the treatment of the dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws rarely dislocate slightly.
And the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways. For either they are moved from their places slightly, and are softened for a while. Or they are dislocated completely and entirely until they soften toward the chest, until the saliva of the patient runs out and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
When the dislocation is slight, then it often returns to its proper position on its own with little intervention.
If indeed there is a complete and total dislocation, then it is necessary that the reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and this is how it should be done, the assistant should hold the patient's head: and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the jaw dislocation between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he should insert two of his thumbs simultaneously, if the dislocation is on both sides. And the other fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he equalizes.
And instruct the patient to clean his jaws and prepare them for eating on all sides. And the physician should equalize the jaw and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated at the same time: then administer fumigation with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy: do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When they return and are equalized, and the mouth of the patient is closed, and they do not soften: then place upon them cloths with plaster made from wax and rose oil.
Then it should be bound with soft bands with ease.
And let the patient sleep on his back: and let his head stand between two pillows so that he does not move it to the right or left: nor should he attempt, or be forced to chew anything: rather, let his food be given by gentle sipping until the pain is removed, and the jaw is set: then let him eat what he wishes, and let it be administered with ease.
Let not the operator work on the mouth's operation concerning eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then fevers often result from this, and constant thirst: and perhaps the stomach of the patient may loosen, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
When therefore you see that: then know that it is a loss, and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection with the breastbone. And it sometimes dislocates outward: and this is evident to the senses.
And its restoration is for the patient to lie on their back and extend their arms. Then compress the area firmly with your palm, for it will return to its place.
Then apply a plaster and poultice over it and bind it tightly.
The ends of the humerus that follow the elbow and are connected to it do not dislocate, except rarely. If it dislocates at some time, then it is necessary to reduce and align it as we have said, and prepare it accordingly.
Then place over it a plaster, and powder, and a bandage. And instruct the sick person to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
And with this same method the arm extremity is reduced when it is also displaced from its place.
On the reduction of dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus does not dislocate except in three ways.
One of which is that it dislocates toward the outer lower part. The second is that it dislocates toward the chest. And it may also dislocate toward the upper part of the humerus: and this rarely occurs.
And it does not dislocate toward the back, because of the spur. And it does not dislocate toward the front, because of the nerves.
And it dislocates and protrudes most toward the lower outer part, especially in those whose flesh is sparse. For it exits quickly in them and enters quickly.
In those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely that it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And it may happen that some people suffer a blow or fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot swelling, and it is assumed because of this that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you test it until you stand upon its certainty, and then proceed in its cure.
And the dislocation is known when it is turned downward towards the armpit: and you distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy shoulder. For you find a clear difference between them, and you find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and below the armpit upon touching the head of the humerus it feels almost like an egg: and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all ways.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards: then you find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is to have the assistant raise the hand upwards. Then place your thumbs below its armpit, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the assistant raises his hand and extends it upwards. Then place it below, for it returns quickly.
If it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation first occurred: then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and a massage that softens and soothes should be administered, such as when the roots of coltsfoot, fenugreek, and sweet clover are boiled in water, and applied.
Then the patient should lie on his back: and a sphere of wool, which is equal in softness and tightness, should be placed under his buttocks.
Then the doctor should place his heel on the sphere, and lift the head of the shoulder with force, and pull the patient's hand towards the lower part.
And if you wish, return him according to this method: and it is that you should have present a man who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on the side, and insert his shoulder under the buttocks of the patient: and lift his buttocks upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another assistant should pull the patient's hand towards the lower part of his abdomen. And if the patient is light: then it is necessary to suspend something else with him to weigh him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced by another method: and it is that a long piece of wood should be fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape: like the sleeve of a mortar: which should not be thick nor thin. Then place under the patient, after soft cloths are put on top of the head of the wood, and let the patient stand along the length of the wood.
Then extend his hand downwards from one side, and stretch his body from the other side with strength: for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If the reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: take a piece of wood whose length is the amount of two arms, and its width the amount of four fingers, and its thickness the amount of two fingers: which has a round head, so as to ease its entry into the deep wound, according to this form.
Then soft cloths should be tied over the round head, so that the wood does not harm the weak area.
Then it should be placed under the wounded area of the patient, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wood down to the lower parts: and the wood should be bound over the support and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a step of a ladder by its width, and the hand should be extended downward, and the rest of the body should be left suspended from the other side. For the joint moves immediately.
Therefore, when its insertion is completed in whatever way possible, then it is necessary to place a sphere of equal size made of wool under the wound. Then apply a plaster prepared from mill dust with frankincense, and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is spread from above.
Then bind under the wound with a suitable bandage: and wrap the bandage over the plaster from above: and attach his hand to his neck, and leave it, without moving his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be reduced until the limb is strengthened: for it is faster for its healing.
Then it should be enlarged after seven days, or five, and exercised with movement. If therefore it is firm and does not soften, then it is already healed.
If however the joint is dislocated frequently, due to moisture that affects it, or for another reason: then it is necessary to apply a cautery with three holes to it, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cauteries.
If all this is done, and the ligament loosens after seven days, and the joint does not strengthen, and the plaster and binding are reapplied several times, and it still does not firm up, and falls off, and cannot lift itself upwards: then you know that the nerves which are at the head of the humerus have either been cut, or stretched, or softened: and you know that the joint will never be set in its place.
The reduction of a dislocation which occurs towards the chest and nipple, or towards the back, is performed with force and extension by hand: until it returns to place, and then the rest of the binding and care should be applied until it heals.
If after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use a bath frequently until that heaviness is alleviated, and it returns to its original nature.
Regarding the treatment of the dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and likewise returns with difficulty as well: and it is dislocated in all directions, but particularly towards the front or the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: for it falls under sight and touch, according to whatever shape it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is clearly shown to you. For the joint is concave, and it cannot bend the arm, nor touch its shoulder with it.
And you must hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess occurs.
For if a hot abscess occurs, the reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all other forms of dislocation, and involves more intense pain: and often death follows with it.
And the restoration of it, when it is one of those that can be reduced, is to extend his hand with two hands: and his arm should be spread out, and the physician's hands should be above and below the elbow, and he should push the joint with his thumbs simultaneously, or with the base of his palms, until it returns to its place.
Or the dislocation is to the front: then it is reduced by bending his hand with his own hand, until he strikes the dislocated elbow with the base of his palm.
If the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer a very strong, powerful extension: and it is done by two assistants extending the arm: and two assistants should also hold it firmly so that it does not move during the extension.
Then the arm is rotated in all directions after a doubled long cloth or a wide bandage is wrapped over his hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil: so that it may be one of those things which help in lubricating the joint with ease. Then push the joint with a forceful thrust until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place on it a plaster that has astringency and drying with egg white: and bind it with a proper tight bandage. And suspend the arm from the patient's neck: and leave it for several days.
Then it should be loosened. If then the joint is stabilized in its place, then loosen the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not well secured, then apply the plaster again, and the bandage, and leave it for several days until it is secured. Then loosen it.
If however heaviness befalls it after the reduction of the joint, and the placement is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and subtle friction, and compression, until it straightens, or place on the joint fat Euphorbia, that is, fat ewe's marrow. Then it should be bandaged, and leave it on it day and night. Then remove it, and insert it into the bath. When therefore he sweats, then friction the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat on the same joint once, and twice, and thrice with each entry to the bath until it is softened.
And if you wish, put cow dung on it, moist and heated with butter, and bind over it.
Do this several times. For it is indeed softened and returns to its normal state.
On the treatment of dislocation by Almahassem.
Almahassem frequently dislocates the hand, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. On the contrary, for the other joints. However, it is necessary that the reduction of its dislocation be swift at the time when it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed or a warm abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is to place the affected hand of Almahassem on a board, and have an assistant extend his hand; and the doctor should place his palm over the prominence of the joint and push it until it returns.
However, it should be considered if the palm has already retreated to the belly of the hand: then the patient should place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction; and if the dislocation proceeds toward the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be inward on the board, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the board so that the doctor's hand falls over the prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a plaster soothing the swelling: and leave it, do not repeat that.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you put it back in place after the days have passed, yet the joint remains according to its own curvature: and it does not harm the patient except that the hand becomes weak, and cannot grasp anything: then you know that the nerves are cut or worn.
There is therefore no remedy for it except to be treated with a cautery near the site of the injury. And perhaps it does not help it at all.
Therefore, when Almahassem does not return, then apply to it the plaster that we have mentioned. Then it should be bound and left for five days: afterwards it should be loosened and left, and the hand should be exercised.
If however its movement is impaired, and something serious occurs in it: then soften it with warm water and rubbing several times until it is softened.
Regarding the treatment of the displacement of fingers.
When one of the fingers is dislocated to any side, or one of the fingers to the back of the palm, or its front: then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then hold the tip of the finger and suspend it toward the side to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then hang him and stretch him until he is straight on his day.
When therefore it will be night, bind him according to this account: and do not cease to loosen him during the day: and exercise him with movement: and bind him at night.
Do this for several days until it is stretched.
And do the same if the hand is dislocated towards the abdomen: bind it to that part and perform your first treatment on it until it heals.
And do the same when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Regarding the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When it happens to someone that there is a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck, or many vertebrae are displaced from their places: then there is no treatment for it, because death approaches the sick person.
And the sign of this is that the excretion of the sick person exits without will, and they cannot hold it in: and often some of their limbs are weakened, or both of their legs, or both of their arms, or one of them: or a vertebra is displaced from its place.
For it often happens that they are displaced to all four parts.
Therefore, that which is displaced backward is called humpback.
The treatment, however, is to consider then, if the humpback occurred from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor any healing at all.
In those that occur from a fall, or blows, or similar things, the ancients have already spoken at length about the methods of treatment. Of which much does not return with profit.
But I have already abbreviated from that, which is little excused by its much due to my closeness to the intention: and in my explanation wherein I explained it, and formed its instrument in a different way from how they showed and explained it.
I say therefore, that in the hump that occurs on the front side, there is no remedy, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which occurs on both sides as well.
And it is not cured by them, except that which occurs on the back properly, with what I narrate: and it is that the patient should be extended on his face on an equal podium near a wall: and a wet straw should be spread under him, so that his chest is not harmed.
Then a straight piece of wood should be placed fixed in a socket in the ground at the end of the podium, near his head: and another piece of wood near his feet at the other end of the podium: and the assistant should hold the wood, and it should not be firmly fixed in the socket. And another assistant should hold the other one.
Then wrap a soft, strong bandage over the patient's head and under their armpits: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood that is by his head: and tie it there.
Then bind another ligature above his thighs, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the ligatures, and tie them to another wooden piece that is at his feet.
Then let all the attendants pull the ligature to the wood part, and do not move the two pieces of wood from the places where they are fixed: however, they should not be firmly fastened, as we said.
And the doctor should place his palms on the spine with force, until it returns; or place a board on it. Then attach his feet to the board until it returns.
If it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And make a hole in the wall of which we spoke that is near the patient, into which the end of the board can be inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the spine. And let the doctor place his feet on the other end. And pull firmly until the spine is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a device that is wrapped around with your hand: and it is such that you fix into the ground near the head of the patient at the end of the footrest two pieces of wood, whose length is one arm's length: and near his head at the end of the footrest two pieces of wood.
And the length which is between all the wood should be one palm: and now make a hole in every place where the turn runs: and secure all the wood very much to the ground so that it does not move at all.
And insert the round wood: and it is a turn where the ligament is rotated in two holes of two woods.
And at its end is a hole, in which the wood is secured, whose length is one palm with that which rotates: and in two other pieces of wood is similarly that.
Then tighten the bands which you have tightened on the chest of the patient to the turn which is at his head: and the bands which you have tightened on his legs to the turn which is at his feet.
Then let a servant stand by each turn, who turns his hand with the leather strap with which the turn is rotated: and the doctor aligns the spine according to what we have said.
This is the form of the turn which follows.
Then after the spine returns and the place is aligned, then it is necessary to apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then apply tow. Then place a strip of board over the plaster, whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length should be the amount that covers the hump and extends over the healthy part of the spine.
Then it should be bound with the band we have mentioned. And let the patient use a subtle nourishment until he is healed.
And if there remains some prominence in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and alleviate, along with the application of the plate we mentioned for a long time. And now it is administered in that sheet of lead.
And sometimes there is a prominence at the last vertebra of the spine: and it is thought that by it there is dislocation, and that bone which now protrudes. Therefore, it is not advisable to administer this medicine to him: for it may cause death.
Regarding the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the joints of the ankle and the shoulder joint only suffer dislocation: they do not experience what happens to other joints due to slight displacement and concavity.
And the joint of the ankle dislocates in four directions. It is because it dislocates towards the front and towards the back.
And it dislocates most towards the inside: and slightly towards the front and the back.
And the treatment for its dislocation towards the inside is as follows: when you align the healthy leg of the patient with the injured one, it appears longer: and his knee protrudes more than that of a healthy person, and the patient cannot bend his foot near the groin: and the area following the groin becomes inflamed with a warm swelling, because the head of the femur has now reached there.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation outward has happened is that its accidents are contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation forward has happened is that he extends the sound leg according to its fullness: however, he does not double it without pain which is in the knee: and if he attempts to walk, he cannot step forward on it: and his urine is retained, and his groin swells, and in walking there is a dropping of his heel.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation backward has happened is that he does not extend the knee, nor can he double it before he doubles the groin: and his leg is even shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip bone is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this species of dislocation is to consider then, if the dislocation is old, which has already become ancient with the one having it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its own disposition: then there is no cure in it at all. Therefore, it should not be entirely presumed for him.
In the case of one whose dislocation is recent, and it is from one of the four species of dislocation: then hasten to this, to rotate the joint, and extend it inward and outward, and move it right and left: for perhaps it will return, and will not require further treatment.
If it does not return this way, then you must prepare a strong assistant, and have the leg extended from the lower part: either with his hands, or with a bandage, which is tied above the leg over the knee: and another assistant should extend his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under the tendons. Then bind with a gentle bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the end of the bandage with the other hand.
And let the extension be either from the front part of the groin to the shoulder part, or from the back part to the dorsal part.
And let the extension of all be at once, until the sick person is lifted with their body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common type among the four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for every type to do what I will say about the proper cure.
In its proper reduction when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the sick person lie on their healthy side. Then place the bandage over the root of the thigh, between the head of the thigh and the area under the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin area to a higher part of the body, toward the shoulder area.
Then let another strong minister take his arms, and press the thick part of the diseased thigh, and extend outward with a forceful stretch. For it now returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced.
If it slips out of your control and does not obey you for insertion with this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary to bind both feet of the sick person together with a strong yet gentle bandage over both heels and both knees: and let the length of each be four fingers from its counterpart: and let the sick leg be extended two fingers more than the other.
Then let the patient be suspended over a beam in the house: and its length from the ground should be two arms' lengths.
Then instruct a strong youth to support the head of the thigh: and let him be suspended with the patient another one: and let another youth push, supporting the thigh with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
In its return to the proper position, when it is dislocated outward, it is necessary that the patient lie on a raised platform, as we have described in the case of one having a hump. And let the band be tied above his diseased leg and above his chest.
Then two pieces of wood should be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let an additional piece of wood be placed in the middle of the platform, firmly fixed: on which wrapped wet cloths should be placed, so that it does not harm the patient: and let the wood be between his thighs, so that it is not drawn downward during the extension.
Then let all the attendants pull on their respective parts, and the doctor equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it obeys the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the same board and press it as we have said in humpbacked cases, equally. However, it is necessary that the patient lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the limb of the patient be extended quickly: and this is according to the same account on a platform: and the physician should place the palm of his right hand upon the groin of the patient. Then he should compress it with the other hand, and with that compression place it extended downwards to the knee area.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, and he should be raised off the ground: rather he should be placed on a hard surface, as is necessary when his hip is dislocated outwardly, according to what we have said about lying on both hips: and it is on his face, with the ligaments extended: as we have said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board on the place to which the joint protrudes, when the reduction is completed as required.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the patient: and when you see them equal, and the patient can move his leg and stretch it without difficulty: then know that the limb has returned as required: then join the two hips, and apply a poultice, and bind with an imemati, that is, a wheel: so that the hip does not move in any part: and the patient should rest quietly for three days or four.
Then loosen the bandage and the poultice, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in standing, then know that the dislocation is now fixed: therefore allow the patient to walk.
And if you see any softening: then return, and plaster it, and tighten it according to your first tightening: and leave it for three days.
Then loosen it and make him walk slowly on both for several days until he is well strengthened.
On the Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, that is, backward.
And it does not dislocate forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply his leg to his hip. If it does not adhere to the hip: then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is to make the patient sit straight, so that he may extend his leg if he can over it, and press your back against his face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then hold his knee with your palms, and place them straight across his knee on top of it between your fingers. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another assistant should extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction is that the leg adheres to the hip with ease, not laboriously.
Then apply plasters to it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with wool for three days, or four. Then loosen them.
And the patient should not use walking for several days until healed.
If the reduction of it resists you, then administer strong extension with ligaments, the use of which was previously described in the treatment of ankylosis, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the calcaneus.
The calcaneus is sometimes displaced slightly, and sometimes dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the calcaneus swollen, protruding to the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its displacement is an easy reduction: it is to be extended with ease by hand and equalized until it returns.
In the treatment of it when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary to make the patient sit straight: and let a strong attendant hold him firmly from the back part of his spine, in the middle of it.
Then hold with your right hand his foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the site of the spur.
Then pull his foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the sole of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have mentioned.
Then push the sole of the foot towards the leg on the third attempt, and you pull out the spur.
If therefore it returns in one attempt, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot even, it is good: and if not, then repeat the procedure on it: because it will return.
If the reduction is prevented by what we have mentioned: then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and fix a stake in the ground very firmly, so that it falls between his thighs: and you now wrap a cloth over it, so that it does not harm the patient.
Then the assistant should take hold of his thigh: afterwards the assistant should extend the other foot, either with his hands, or with a band that is tied above the ankle. Then the assistant should extend against the extension of his patient: and the stake should stand between the two thighs of the patient holding him so that his body is not drawn downwards during the extension.
Then the doctor should equalize the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant should hold the healthy leg at the lower part. For the dislocation returns with speed.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and the health of its restoration is declared to you: then apply a poultice and tow, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And you must be cautious or fearful of the nerves that are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not too forceful over them and injures them.
Then leave it for two days or three. If the bandage softens, bind it tightly: then loosen it on the third or fourth day: and prevent the patient from walking for forty days. If however he attempts to walk before that period, there is no security that the dislocation will not be dissolved over him, and be corrupted, and not receive healing after that.
If a warm abscess should occur to him, then it is necessary to administer in its reduction what was previously described by our account in another place concerning treatment and embrocation, until it is removed.
Regarding the dislocation of the toes and its treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated among them be aligned with slight extension without difficulty: and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the foot's arch: then it is necessary that the patient sit with his foot on an even place on the ground, or on a board: and he should stand as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it with your foot forcefully until it returns and you see it now even, and no protrusion appears in the place.
Then place under the arch of his foot a board that captures the entire foot, which has two ends. Then bind it tightly for three days. Then release it, and have him rest from walking for many days, until it is tightened and secured from displacement.
Regarding the types of dislocations with wounds or fractures.
When something of this kind occurs and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often follows: and for this reason one should not presume to treat such a condition unless he is wise in the art through long experience, merciful, pious, cautious, not bold, nor presumptuous: and that he use at the outset only medicines which calm hot swellings: and leave the patient to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for recovery from peril with the mildness of the illness, and in which there appears some hope. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns as we wish, then administer treatment that calms the swelling: and treat the wound with a drying ointment suitable for it.
If there is dislocation with fracture, and saprae separate accidents occur in the bone, then attempt their removal, and do what we said for simple ailments resulting from that which precedes in their locations.
And be careful according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the way of fear: according to what my previous teaching has told you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more beneficial to your body among them in your time.
